Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone
J. K. Rowling


Harry Potter #1


Harry Potter and the Sorcererís Stone


For Jessica, who loves stories,

For Anne, who loved them too;

And for Di, who heard this one first.





1. THE BOY WHO LIVED


Mr. and Mrs. Dursley, of number four, Privet Drive, were proud to say that they were perfectly normal, thank you very much. They were the last people youíd expect to be involved in anything strange or mysterious, because they just didnít hold with such nonsense.

Mr. Dursley was the director of a firm called Grunnings, which made drills. He was a big, beefy man with hardly any neck, although he did have a very large mustache. Mrs. Dursley was thin and blonde and had nearly twice the usual amount of neck, which came in very useful as she spent so much of her time craning over garden fences, spying on the neighbors. The Dursleys had a small son called Dudley and in their opinion there was no finer boy anywhere.

The Dursleys had everything they wanted, but they also had a secret, and their greatest fear was that somebody would discover it. They didnít think they could bear it if anyone found out about the Potters. Mrs. Potter was Mrs. Dursleyís sister, but they hadnít met for several years; in fact, Mrs. Dursley pretended she didnít have a sister, because her sister and her good for nothing husband were as unDursleyish as it was possible to be. The Dursleys shuddered to think what the neighbors would say if the Potters arrived in the street. The Dursleys knew that the Potters had a small son, too, but they had never even seen him. This boy was another good reason for keeping the Potters away; they didnít want Dudley mixing with a child like that.

When Mr. and Mrs. Dursley woke up on the dull, gray Tuesday our story starts, there was nothing about the cloudy sky outside to suggest that strange and mysterious things would soon be happening all over the country. Mr. Dursley hummed as he picked out his most boring tie for work, and Mrs. Dursley gossiped away happily as she wrestled a screaming Dudley into his high chair.

None of them noticed a large, tawny owl flutter past the window.

At half past eight, Mr. Dursley picked up his briefcase, pecked Mrs. Dursley on the cheek, and tried to kiss Dudley good bye but missed, because Dudley was now having a tantrum and throwing his cereal at the walls.

ìLittle tyke,î chortled Mr. Dursley as he left the house. He got into his car and backed out of number fourís drive.

It was on the corner of the street that he noticed the first sign of something peculiaróa cat reading a map. For a second, Mr. Dursley didnít realize what he had seenóthen he jerked his head around to look again. There was a tabby cat standing on the corner of Privet Drive, but there wasnít a map in sight. What could he have been thinking of? It must have been a trick of the light. Mr. Dursley blinked and stared at the cat. It stared back. As Mr. Dursley drove around the corner and up the road, he watched the cat in his mirror. It was now reading the sign that said Privet Driveóno, looking at the sign; cats couldnít read maps or signs. Mr. Dursley gave himself a little shake and put the cat out of his mind. As he drove toward town he thought of nothing except a large order of drills he was hoping to get that day.

But on the edge of town, drills were driven out of his mind by something else. As he sat in the usual morning traffic jam, he couldnít help noticing that there seemed to be a lot of strangely dressed people about. People in cloaks. Mr. Dursley couldnít bear people who dressed in funny clothesóthe getups you saw on young people! He supposed this was some stupid new fashion. He drummed his fingers on the steering wheel and his eyes fell on a huddle of these weirdos standing quite close by. They were whispering excitedly together. Mr. Dursley was enraged to see that a couple of them werenít young at all; why, that man had to be older than he was, and wearing an emerald green cloak! The nerve of him! But then it struck Mr. Dursley that this was probably some silly stuntóthese people were obviously collecting for somethingÖ yes, that would be it. The traffic moved on and a few minutes later, Mr. Dursley arrived in the Grunnings parking lot, his mind back on drills.

Mr. Dursley always sat with his back to the window in his office on the ninth floor. If he hadnít, he might have found it harder to concentrate on drills that morning. He didnít see the owls swooping past in broad daylight, though people down in the street did; they pointed and gazed open-mouthed as owl after owl sped overhead. Most of them had never seen an owl even at nighttime. Mr. Dursley, however, had a perfectly normal, owl free morning. He yelled at five different people. He made several important telephone calls and shouted a bit more. He was in a very good mood until lunchtime, when he thought heíd stretch his legs and walk across the road to buy himself a bun from the bakery.

Heíd forgotten all about the people in cloaks until he passed a group of them next to the bakerís. He eyed them angrily as he passed. He didnít know why, but they made him uneasy. This bunch were whispering excitedly, too, and he couldnít see a single collecting tin. It was on his way back past them, clutching a large doughnut in a bag, that he caught a few words of what they were saying.

ìThe Potters, thatís right, thatís what I heardóî

ìóyes, their son, Harryóî

Mr. Dursley stopped dead. Fear flooded him. He looked back at the whisperers as if he wanted to say something to them, but thought better of it.

He dashed back across the road, hurried up to his office, snapped at his secretary not to disturb him, seized his telephone, and had almost finished dialing his home number when he changed his mind. He put the receiver back down and stroked his mustache, thinkingÖ no, he was being stupid. Potter wasnít such an unusual name. He was sure there were lots of people called Potter who had a son called Harry. Come to think of it, he wasnít even sure his nephew was called Harry. Heíd never even seen the boy. It might have been Harvey. Or Harold. There was no point in worrying Mrs. Dursley; she always got so upset at any mention of her sister. He didnít blame heróif heíd had a sister like thatÖ but all the same, those people in cloaksÖ

He found it a lot harder to concentrate on drills that afternoon and when he left the building at five oíclock, he was still so worried that he walked straight into someone just outside the door.

ìSorry,î he grunted, as the tiny old man stumbled and almost fell. It was a few seconds before Mr. Dursley realized that the man was wearing a violet cloak. He didnít seem at all upset at being almost knocked to the ground. On the contrary, his face split into a wide smile and he said in a squeaky voice that made passersby stare, ìDonít be sorry, my dear sir, for nothing could upset me today! Rejoice, for You-Know-Who has gone at last! Even Muggles like yourself should be celebrating, this happy, happy day!î

And the old man hugged Mr. Dursley around the middle and walked off.

Mr. Dursley stood rooted to the spot. He had been hugged by a complete stranger. He also thought he had been called a Muggle, whatever that was. He was rattled. He hurried to his car and set off for home, hoping he was imagining things, which he had never hoped before, because he didnít approve of imagination.

As he pulled into the driveway of number four, the first thing he sawóand it didnít improve his moodówas the tabby cat heíd spotted that morning. It was now sitting on his garden wall. He was sure it was the same one; it had the same markings around its eyes.

ìShoo!î said Mr. Dursley loudly.

The cat didnít move. It just gave him a stern look. Was this normal cat behavior? Mr. Dursley wondered. Trying to pull himself together, he let himself into the house. He was still determined not to mention anything to his wife.

Mrs. Dursley had had a nice, normal day. She told him over dinner all about Mrs. Next Doorís problems with her daughter and how Dudley had learned a new word (ìWonít!î). Mr. Dursley tried to act normally. When Dudley had been put to bed, he went into the living room in time to catch the last report on the evening news:

ìAnd finally, bird watchers everywhere have reported that the nationís owls have been behaving very unusually today. Although owls normally hunt at night and are hardly ever seen in daylight, there have been hundreds of sightings of these birds flying in every direction since sunrise. Experts are unable to explain why the owls have suddenly changed their sleeping pattern.î The newscaster allowed himself a grin. ìMost mysterious. And now, over to Jim McGuffin with the weather. Going to be any more showers of owls tonight, Jim?î

ìWell, Ted,î said the weatherman, ìI donít know about that, but itís not only the owls that have been acting oddly today. Viewers as far apart as Kent, Yorkshire, and Dundee have been phoning in to tell me that instead of the rain I promised yesterday, theyíve had a downpour of shooting stars! Perhaps people have been celebrating Bonfire Night earlyóitís not until next week, folks! But I can promise a wet night tonight.î

Mr. Dursley sat frozen in his armchair. Shooting stars all over Britain? Owls flying by daylight? Mysterious people in cloaks all over the place? And a whisper, a whisper about the PottersÖ

Mrs. Dursley came into the living room carrying two cups of tea. It was no good. Heíd have to say something to her. He cleared his throat nervously. ìEróPetunia, dearóyou havenít heard from your sister lately, have you?î

As he had expected, Mrs. Dursley looked shocked and angry. After all, they normally pretended she didnít have a sister.

ìNo,î she said sharply. ìWhy?î

ìFunny stuff on the news,î Mr. Dursley mumbled. ìOwlsÖ shooting starsÖ and there were a lot of funny looking people in town todayÖî

ìSo?î snapped Mrs. Dursley.

ìWell, I just thoughtÖ maybeÖ it was something to do withÖ you knowÖ her crowd.î

Mrs. Dursley sipped her tea through pursed lips. Mr. Dursley wondered whether he dared tell her heíd heard the name ìPotter.î He decided he didnít dare. Instead he said, as casually as he could, ìTheir sonóheíd be about Dudleyís age now, wouldnít he?î

ìI suppose so,î said Mrs. Dursley stiffly.

ìWhatís his name again? Howard, isnít it?î

ìHarry. Nasty, common name, if you ask me.î

ìOh, yes,î said Mr. Dursley, his heart sinking horribly. ìYes, I quite agree.î

He didnít say another word on the subject as they went upstairs to bed. While Mrs. Dursley was in the bathroom, Mr. Dursley crept to the bedroom window and peered down into the front garden. The cat was still there. It was staring down Privet Drive as though it were waiting for something.

Was he imagining things? Could all this have anything to do with the Potters? If it didÖ if it got out that they were related to a pair ofówell, he didnít think he could bear it.

The Dursleys got into bed. Mrs. Dursley fell asleep quickly but Mr. Dursley lay awake, turning it all over in his mind. His last, comforting thought before he fell asleep was that even if the Potters were involved, there was no reason for them to come near him and Mrs. Dursley. The Potters knew very well what he and Petunia thought about them and their kindÖ He couldnít see how he and Petunia could get mixed up in anything that might be going onóhe yawned and turned overóit couldnít affect themÖ

How very wrong he was.

Mr. Dursley might have been drifting into an uneasy sleep, but the cat on the wall outside was showing no sign of sleepiness. It was sitting as still as a statue, its eyes fixed unblinkingly on the far corner of Privet Drive. It didnít so much as quiver when a car door slammed on the next street, nor when two owls swooped overhead. In fact, it was nearly midnight before the cat moved at all.

A man appeared on the corner the cat had been watching, appeared so suddenly and silently youíd have thought heíd just popped out of the ground. The catís tail twitched and its eyes narrowed.

Nothing like this man had ever been seen on Privet Drive. He was tall, thin, and very old, judging by the silver of his hair and beard, which were both long enough to tuck into his belt. He was wearing long robes, a purple cloak that swept the ground, and high heeled, buckled boots. His blue eyes were light, bright, and sparkling behind half moon spectacles and his nose was very long and crooked, as though it had been broken at least twice. This manís name was Albus Dumbledore.

Albus Dumbledore didnít seem to realize that he had just arrived in a street where everything from his name to his boots was unwelcome. He was busy rummaging in his cloak, looking for something. But he did seem to realize he was being watched, because he looked up suddenly at the cat, which was still staring at him from the other end of the street. For some reason, the sight of the cat seemed to amuse him. He chuckled and muttered, ìI should have known.î

He found what he was looking for in his inside pocket. It seemed to be a silver cigarette lighter. He flicked it open, held it up in the air, and clicked it. The nearest street lamp went out with a little pop. He clicked it againóthe next lamp flickered into darkness. Twelve times he clicked the Put Outer, until the only lights left on the whole street were two tiny pinpricks in the distance, which were the eyes of the cat watching him. If anyone looked out of their window now, even beady eyed Mrs. Dursley, they wouldnít be able to see anything that was happening down on the pavement. Dumbledore slipped the Put Outer back inside his cloak and set off down the street toward number four, where he sat down on the wall next to the cat. He didnít look at it, but after a moment he spoke to it.

ìFancy seeing you here, Professor McGonagall.î

He turned to smile at the tabby, but it had gone. Instead he was smiling at a rather severe looking woman who was wearing square glasses exactly the shape of the markings the cat had had around its eyes. She, too, was wearing a cloak, an emerald one. Her black hair was drawn into a tight bun. She looked distinctly ruffled.

ìHow did you know it was me?î she asked.

ìMy dear Professor, Iíve never seen a cat sit so stiffly.î

ìYouíd be stiff if youíd been sitting on a brick wall all day,î said Professor McGonagall.

ìAll day? When you could have been celebrating? I must have passed a dozen feasts and parties on my way here.î

Professor McGonagall sniffed angrily.

ìOh yes, everyoneís celebrating, all right,î she said impatiently. ìYouíd think theyíd be a bit more careful, but noóeven the Muggles have noticed somethingís going on. It was on their news.î She jerked her head back at the Dursleysí dark living room window. ìI heard it. Flocks of owlsÖ shooting starsÖ Well, theyíre not completely stupid. They were bound to notice something. Shooting stars down in KentóIíll bet that was Dedalus Diggle. He never had much sense.î

ìYou canít blame them,î said Dumbledore gently. ìWeíve had precious little to celebrate for eleven years.î

ìI know that,î said Professor McGonagall irritably. ìBut thatís no reason to lose our heads. People are being downright careless, out on the streets in broad daylight, not even dressed in Muggle clothes, swapping rumors.î

She threw a sharp, sideways glance at Dumbledore here, as though hoping he was going to tell her something, but he didnít, so she went on. ìA fine thing it would be if, on the very day You-Know-Who seems to have disappeared at last, the Muggles found out about us all. I suppose he really has gone, Dumbledore?î

ìIt certainly seems so,î said Dumbledore. ìWe have much to be thankful for. Would you care for a lemon drop?î

ìA what?î

ìA lemon drop. Theyíre a kind of Muggle sweet Iím rather fond of.î

ìNo, thank you,î said Professor McGonagall coldly, as though she didnít think this was the moment for lemon drops. ìAs I say, even if You-Know-Who has goneóî

ìMy dear Professor, surely a sensible person like yourself can call him by his name? All this ëYou-Know-Whoí nonsenseófor eleven years I have been trying to persuade people to call him by his proper name: Voldemort.î Professor McGonagall flinched, but Dumbledore, who was unsticking two lemon drops, seemed not to notice. ìIt all gets so confusing if we keep saying ëYou-Know-Who.í I have never seen any reason to be frightened of saying Voldemortís name.î

ìI know you havenítî, said Professor McGonagall, sounding half exasperated, half admiring. ìBut youíre different. Everyone knows youíre the only one You Knowóoh, all right, Voldemort, was frightened of.î

ìYou flatter me,î said Dumbledore calmly. ìVoldemort had powers I will never have.î

ìOnly because youíre tooówellónoble to use them.î

ìItís lucky itís dark. I havenít blushed so much since Madam Pomfrey told me she liked my new earmuffs.î

Professor McGonagall shot a sharp look at Dumbledore and said, ìThe owls are nothing next to the rumors that are flying around. You know what everyoneís saying? About why heís disappeared? About what finally stopped him?î

It seemed that Professor McGonagall had reached the point she was most anxious to discuss, the real reason she had been waiting on a cold, hard wall all day, for neither as a cat nor as a woman had she fixed Dumbledore with such a piercing stare as she did now. It was plain that whatever ìeveryoneî was saying, she was not going to believe it until Dumbledore told her it was true. Dumbledore, however, was choosing another lemon drop and did not answer.

ìWhat theyíresaying,î she pressed on, ìis that last night Voldemort turned up in Godricís Hollow. He went to find the Potters. The rumor is that Lily and James Potter areóareóthat theyíreódead.î

Dumbledore bowed his head. Professor McGonagall gasped.

ìLily and JamesÖ I canít believe itÖ I didnít want to believe itÖ Oh, AlbusÖî

Dumbledore reached out and patted her on the shoulder. ìI knowÖ I knowÖî he said heavily.

Professor McGonagallís voice trembled as she went on. ìThatís not all. Theyíre saying he tried to kill the Potterís son, Harry. Butóhe couldnít. He couldnít kill that little boy. No one knows why, or how, but theyíre saying that when he couldnít kill Harry Potter, Voldemortís power somehow brokeóand thatís why heís gone.î

Dumbledore nodded glumly.

ìItísóitís true?î faltered Professor McGonagall. ìAfter all heís doneÖ all the people heís killedÖ he couldnít kill a little boy? Itís just astoundingÖ of all the things to stop himÖ but how in the name of heaven did Harry survive?î

ìWe can only guess,î said Dumbledore. ìWe may never know.î

Professor McGonagall pulled out a lace handkerchief and dabbed at her eyes beneath her spectacles. Dumbledore gave a great sniff as he took a golden watch from his pocket and examined it. It was a very odd watch. It had twelve hands but no numbers; instead, little planets were moving around the edge. It must have made sense to Dumbledore, though, because he put it back in his pocket and said, ìHagridís late. I suppose it was he who told you Iíd be here, by the way?î

ìYes,î said Professor McGonagall. ìAnd I donít suppose youíre going to tell me why youíre here, of all places?î

ìIíve come to bring Harry to his aunt and uncle. Theyíre the only family he has left now.î

ìYou donít meanóyou canít mean the people who live here?î cried Professor McGonagall, jumping to her feet and pointing at number four. ìDumbledoreóyou canít. Iíve been watching them all day. You couldnít find two people who are less like us. And theyíve got this sonóI saw him kicking his mother all the way up the street, screaming for sweets. Harry Potter come and live here!î

ìItís the best place for him,î said Dumbledore firmly. ìHis aunt and uncle will be able to explain everything to him when heís older. Iíve written them a letter.î

ìA letter?î repeated Professor McGonagall faintly, sitting back down on the wall. ìReally, Dumbledore, you think you can explain all this in a letter? These people will never understand him! Heíll be famousóa legendóI wouldnít be surprised if today was known as Harry Potter day in the futureóthere will be books written about Harryóevery child in our world will know his name!î

ìExactly,î said Dumbledore, looking very seriously over the top of his half moon glasses. ìIt would be enough to turn any boyís head. Famous before he can walk and talk! Famous for something he wonít even remember! Canít you see how much better off heíll be, growing up away from all that until heís ready to take it?î

Professor McGonagall opened her mouth, changed her mind, swallowed, and then said, ìYesóyes, youíre right, of course. But how is the boy getting here, Dumbledore?î She eyed his cloak suddenly as though she thought he might be hiding Harry underneath it.

ìHagridís bringing him.î

ìYou think itówiseóto trust Hagrid with something as important as this?î

ìI would trust Hagrid with my life,î said Dumbledore.

ìIím not saying his heart isnít in the right place,î said Professor McGonagall grudgingly, ìbut you canít pretend heís not careless. He does tend toówhat was that?î

A low rumbling sound had broken the silence around them. It grew steadily louder as they looked up and down the street for some sign of a headlight; it swelled to a roar as they both looked up at the skyóand a huge motorcycle fell out of the air and landed on the road in front of them.

If the motorcycle was huge, it was nothing to the man sitting astride it. He was almost twice as tall as a normal man and at least five times as wide. He looked simply too big to be allowed, and so wildólong tangles of bushy black hair and beard hid most of his face, he had hands the size of trash can lids, and his feet in their leather boots were like baby dolphins. In his vast, muscular arms he was holding a bundle of blankets.

ìHagrid,î said Dumbledore, sounding relieved. ìAt last. And where did you get that motorcycle?î

ìBorrowed it, Professor Dumbledore, sir,î said the giant, climbing carefully off the motorcycle as he spoke. ìYoung Sirius Black lent it to me. Iíve got him, sir.î

ìNo problems, were there?î

ìNo, siróhouse was almost destroyed, but I got him out all right before the Muggles started swarminí around. He fell asleep as we was flyiní over Bristol.î

Dumbledore and Professor McGonagall bent forward over the bundle of blankets. Inside, just visible, was a baby boy, fast asleep. Under a tuft of jet black hair over his forehead they could see a curiously shaped cut, like a bolt of lightning.

ìIs that whereó?î whispered Professor McGonagall.

ìYes,î said Dumbledore. ìHeíll have that scar forever.î

ìCouldnít you do something about it, Dumbledore?î

ìEven if I could, I wouldnít. Scars can come in handy. I have one myself above my left knee that is a perfect map of the London Underground. Wellógive him here, Hagridóweíd better get this over with.î

Dumbledore took Harry in his arms and turned toward the Dursleysí house.

ìCould Iócould I say good bye to him, sir?î asked Hagrid. He bent his great, shaggy head over Harry and gave him what must have been a very scratchy, whiskery kiss. Then, suddenly, Hagrid let out a howl like a wounded dog.

ìShhh!î hissed Professor McGonagall, ìyouíll wake the Muggles!î

ìS-s-sorry,î sobbed Hagrid, taking out a large, spotted handkerchief and burying his face in it. ìBut I c-c-canít stand itóLily aní James deadóaní poor little Harry off ter live with Mugglesóî

ìYes, yes, itís all very sad, but get a grip on yourself, Hagrid, or weíll be found,î Professor McGonagall whispered, patting Hagrid gingerly on the arm as Dumbledore stepped over the low garden wall and walked to the front door. He laid Harry gently on the doorstep, took a letter out of his cloak, tucked it inside Harryís blankets, and then came back to the other two. For a full minute the three of them stood and looked at the little bundle; Hagridís shoulders shook, Professor McGonagall blinked furiously, and the twinkling light that usually shone from Dumbledoreís eyes seemed to have gone out.

ìWell,î said Dumbledore finally, ìthatís that. Weíve no business staying here. We may as well go and join the celebrations.î

ìYeah,î said Hagrid in a very muffled voice, ìIíll be takiní Sirius his bike back. Gínight, Professor McGonagallóProfessor Dumbledore, sir.î

Wiping his streaming eyes on his jacket sleeve, Hagrid swung himself onto the motorcycle and kicked the engine into life; with a roar it rose into the air and off into the night.

ìI shall see you soon, I expect, Professor McGonagall,î said Dumbledore, nodding to her. Professor McGonagall blew her nose in reply.

Dumbledore turned and walked back down the street. On the corner he stopped and took out the silver Put-Outer. He clicked it once, and twelve balls of light sped back to their street lamps so that Privet Drive glowed suddenly orange and he could make out a tabby cat slinking around the corner at the other end of the street. He could just see the bundle of blankets on the step of number four.

ìGood luck, Harry,î he murmured. He turned on his heel and with a swish of his cloak, he was gone.

A breeze ruffled the neat hedges of Privet Drive, which lay silent and tidy under the inky sky, the very last place you would expect astonishing things to happen. Harry Potter rolled over inside his blankets without waking up. One small hand closed on the letter beside him and he slept on, not knowing he was special, not knowing he was famous, not knowing he would be woken in a few hoursí time by Mrs. Dursleyís scream as she opened the front door to put out the milk bottles, nor that he would spend the next few weeks being prodded and pinched by his cousin DudleyÖ He couldnít know that at this very moment, people meeting in secret all over the country were holding up their glasses and saying in hushed voices: ìTo Harry Potteróthe boy who lived!î




2. THE VANISHING GLASS


Nearly ten years had passed since the Dursleys had woken up to find their nephew on the front step, but Privet Drive had hardly changed at all. The sun rose on the same tidy front gardens and lit up the brass number four on the Dursleysí front door; it crept into their living room, which was almost exactly the same as it had been on the night when Mr. Dursley had seen that fateful news report about the owls. Only the photographs on the mantelpiece really showed how much time had passed. Ten years ago, there had been lots of pictures of what looked like a large pink beach ball wearing different colored bonnetsóbut Dudley Dursley was no longer a baby, and now the photographs showed a large blond boy riding his first bicycle, on a carousel at the fair, playing a computer game with his father, being hugged and kissed by his mother. The room held no sign at all that another boy lived in the house, too.

Yet Harry Potter was still there, asleep at the moment, but not for long. His Aunt Petunia was awake and it was her shrill voice that made the first noise of the day.

ìUp! Get up! Now!î

Harry woke with a start. His aunt rapped on the door again.

ìUp!î she screeched. Harry heard her walking toward the kitchen and then the sound of the frying pan being put on the stove. He rolled onto his back and tried to remember the dream he had been having. It had been a good one. There had been a flying motorcycle in it. He had a funny feeling heíd had the same dream before.

His aunt was back outside the door.

ìAre you up yet?î she demanded.

ìNearly,î said Harry.

ìWell, get a move on, I want you to look after the bacon. And donít you dare let it burn, I want everything perfect on Duddyís birthday.î

Harry groaned.

ìWhat did you say?î his aunt snapped through the door.

ìNothing, nothingÖî

Dudleyís birthdayóhow could he have forgotten? Harry got slowly out of bed and started looking for socks. He found a pair under his bed and, after pulling a spider off one of them, put them on. Harry was used to spiders, because the cupboard under the stairs was full of them, and that was where he slept.

When he was dressed he went down the hall into the kitchen. The table was almost hidden beneath all Dudleyís birthday presents. It looked as though Dudley had gotten the new computer he wanted, not to mention the second television and the racing bike. Exactly why Dudley wanted a racing bike was a mystery to Harry, as Dudley was very fat and hated exerciseóunless of course it involved punching somebody. Dudleyís favorite punching bag was Harry, but he couldnít often catch him. Harry didnít look it, but he was very fast.

Perhaps it had something to do with living in a dark cupboard, but Harry had always been small and skinny for his age. He looked even smaller and skinnier than he really was because all he had to wear were old clothes of Dudleyís, and Dudley was about four times bigger than he was. Harry had a thin face, knobbly knees, black hair, and bright green eyes. He wore round glasses held together with a lot of Scotch tape because of all the times Dudley had punched him on the nose. The only thing Harry liked about his own appearance was a very thin scar on his forehead that was shaped like a bolt of lightning. He had had it as long as he could remember, and the first question he could ever remember asking his Aunt Petunia was how he had gotten it.

ìIn the car crash when your parents died,î she had said. ìAnd donít ask questions.î

Donít ask questionsóthat was the first rule for a quiet life with the Dursleys.

Uncle Vernon entered the kitchen as Harry was turning over the bacon.

ìComb your hair!î he barked, by way of a morning greeting.

About once a week, Uncle Vernon looked over the top of his newspaper and shouted that Harry needed a haircut. Harry must have had more haircuts than the rest of the boys in his class put together, but it made no difference, his hair simply grew that wayóall over the place.

Harry was frying eggs by the time Dudley arrived in the kitchen with his mother. Dudley looked a lot like Uncle Vernon. He had a large pink face, not much neck, small, watery blue eyes, and thick blond hair that lay smoothly on his thick, fat head. Aunt Petunia often said that Dudley looked like a baby angelóHarry often said that Dudley looked like a pig in a wig.

Harry put the plates of egg and bacon on the table, which was difficult as there wasnít much room. Dudley, meanwhile, was counting his presents. His face fell.

ìThirty six,î he said, looking up at his mother and father. ìThatís two less than last year.î

ìDarling, you havenít counted Auntie Margeís present, see, itís here under this big one from Mommy and Daddy.î

ìAll right, thirty seven then,î said Dudley, going red in the face. Harry, who could see a huge Dudley tantrum coming on, began wolfing down his bacon as fast as possible in case Dudley turned the table over.

Aunt Petunia obviously scented danger, too, because she said quickly, ìAnd weíll buy you another two presents while weíre out today. Howís that, popkin? Two more presents. Is that all right?î

Dudley thought for a moment. It looked like hard work. Finally he said slowly, ìSo Iíll have thirty Ö thirtyÖî

ìThirty nine, sweetums,î said Aunt Petunia.

ìOh.î Dudley sat down heavily and grabbed the nearest parcel. ìAll right then.î

Uncle Vernon chuckled. ìLittle tyke wants his moneyís worth, just like his father. íAtta boy, Dudley!î He ruffled Dudleyís hair.

At that moment the telephone rang and Aunt Petunia went to answer it while Harry and Uncle Vernon watched Dudley unwrap the racing bike, a video camera, a remote control airplane, sixteen new computer games, and a VCR. He was ripping the paper off a gold wristwatch when Aunt Petunia came back from the telephone looking both angry and worried.

ìBad news, Vernon,î she said. ìMrs. Figgís broken her leg. She canít take him.î She jerked her head in Harryís direction.

Dudleyís mouth fell open in horror, but Harryís heart gave a leap. Every year on Dudleyís birthday, his parents took him and a friend out for the day, to adventure parks, hamburger restaurants, or the movies. Every year, Harry was left behind with Mrs. Figg, a mad old lady who lived two streets away. Harry hated it there. The whole house smelled of cabbage and Mrs. Figg made him look at photographs of all the cats sheíd ever owned.

ìNow what?î said Aunt Petunia, looking furiously at Harry as though heíd planned this. Harry knew he ought to feel sorry that Mrs. Figg had broken her leg, but it wasnít easy when he reminded himself it would be a whole year before he had to look at Tibbles, Snowy, Mr. Paws, and Tufty again.

ìWe could phone Marge,î Uncle Vernon suggested.

ìDonít be silly, Vernon, she hates the boy.î

The Dursleys often spoke about Harry like this, as though he wasnít thereóor rather, as though he was something very nasty that couldnít understand them, like a slug.

ìWhat about whatís her name, your friendóYvonne?î

ìOn vacation in Majorca,î snapped Aunt Petunia.

ìYou could just leave me here,î Harry put in hopefully (heíd be able to watch what he wanted on television for a change and maybe even have a go on Dudleyís computer).

Aunt Petunia looked as though sheíd just swallowed a lemon.

ìAnd come back and find the house in ruins?î she snarled.

ìI wonít blow up the house,î said Harry, but they werenít listening.

ìI suppose we could take him to the zoo,î said Aunt Petunia slowly, ìÖand leave him in the carÖî

ìThat carís new, heís not sitting in it aloneÖî

Dudley began to cry loudly. In fact, he wasnít really cryingóit had been years since heíd really criedóbut he knew that if he screwed up his face and wailed, his mother would give him anything he wanted.

ìDinky Duddydums, donít cry, Mummy wonít let him spoil your special day!î she cried, flinging her arms around him.

ìIÖ donítÖ wantÖ himÖ t-t-to come!î Dudley yelled between huge, pretend sobs. ìHe always sp-spoils everything!î He shot Harry a nasty grin through the gap in his motherís arms.

Just then, the doorbell rangóìOh, good Lord, theyíre here!î said Aunt Petunia franticallyóand a moment later, Dudleyís best friend, Piers Polkiss, walked in with his mother. Piers was a scrawny boy with a face like a rat. He was usually the one who held peopleís arms behind their backs while Dudley hit them. Dudley stopped pretending to cry at once.

Half an hour later, Harry, who couldnít believe his luck, was sitting in the back of the Dursleysí car with Piers and Dudley, on the way to the zoo for the first time in his life. His aunt and uncle hadnít been able to think of anything else to do with him, but before theyíd left, Uncle Vernon had taken Harry aside.

ìIím warning you,î he had said, putting his large purple face right up close to Harryís, ìIím warning you now, boyóany funny business, anything at allóand youíll be in that cupboard from now until Christmas.î

ìIím not going to do anything,î said Harry, ìhonestlyÖî

But Uncle Vernon didnít believe him. No one ever did.

The problem was, strange things often happened around Harry and it was just no good telling the Dursleys he didnít make them happen.

Once, Aunt Petunia, tired of Harry coming back from the barbers looking as though he hadnít been at all, had taken a pair of kitchen scissors and cut his hair so short he was almost bald except for his bangs, which she left ìto hide that horrible scar.î Dudley had laughed himself silly at Harry, who spent a sleepless night imagining school the next day, where he was already laughed at for his baggy clothes and taped glasses. Next morning, however, he had gotten up to find his hair exactly as it had been before Aunt Petunia had sheared it off. He had been given a week in his cupboard for this, even though he had tried to explain that he couldnít explain how it had grown back so quickly.

Another time, Aunt Petunia had been trying to force him into a revolting old sweater of Dudleyís (brown with orange puff balls)óThe harder she tried to pull it over his head, the smaller it seemed to become, until finally it might have fitted a hand puppet, but certainly wouldnít fit Harry. Aunt Petunia had decided it must have shrunk in the wash and, to his great relief, Harry wasnít punished.

On the other hand, heíd gotten into terrible trouble for being found on the roof of the school kitchens. Dudleyís gang had been chasing him as usual when, as much to Harryís surprise as anyone elseís, there he was sitting on the chimney. The Dursleys had received a very angry letter from Harryís headmistress telling them Harry had been climbing school buildings. But all heíd tried to do (as he shouted at Uncle Vernon through the locked door of his cupboard) was jump behind the big trash cans outside the kitchen doors. Harry supposed that the wind must have caught him in mid-jump.

But today, nothing was going to go wrong. It was even worth being with Dudley and Piers to be spending the day somewhere that wasnít school, his cupboard, or Mrs. Figgís cabbage smelling living room.

While he drove, Uncle Vernon complained to Aunt Petunia. He liked to complain about things: people at work, Harry, the council, Harry, the bank, and Harry were just a few of his favorite subjects. This morning, it was motorcycles.

ìÖroaring along like maniacs, the young hoodlums,î he said, as a motorcycle overtook them.

ìI had a dream about a motorcycle,î said Harry, remembering suddenly. ìIt was flying.î

Uncle Vernon nearly crashed into the car in front. He turned right around in his seat and yelled at Harry, his face like a gigantic beet with a mustache: ìMOTORCYCLES DONíT FLY!î

Dudley and Piers sniggered.

ìI know they donít,î said Harry. ìIt was only a dream.î

But he wished he hadnít said anything. If there was one thing the Dursleys hated even more than his asking questions, it was his talking about anything acting in a way it shouldnít, no matter if it was in a dream or even a cartoonóthey seemed to think he might get dangerous ideas.

It was a very sunny Saturday and the zoo was crowded with families. The Dursleys bought Dudley and Piers large chocolate ice creams at the entrance and then, because the smiling lady in the van had asked Harry what he wanted before they could hurry him away, they bought him a cheap lemon ice pop. It wasnít bad, either, Harry thought, licking it as they watched a gorilla scratching its head who looked remarkably like Dudley, except that it wasnít blond.

Harry had the best morning heíd had in a long time. He was careful to walk a little way apart from the Dursleys so that Dudley and Piers, who were starting to get bored with the animals by lunchtime, wouldnít fall back on their favorite hobby of hitting him. They ate in the zoo restaurant, and when Dudley had a tantrum because his knickerbocker glory didnít have enough ice cream on top, Uncle Vernon bought him another one and Harry was allowed to finish the first.

Harry felt, afterward, that he should have known it was all too good to last.

After lunch they went to the reptile house. It was cool and dark in there, with lit windows all along the walls. Behind the glass, all sorts of lizards and snakes were crawling and slithering over bits of wood and stone. Dudley and Piers wanted to see huge, poisonous cobras and thick, man crushing pythons. Dudley quickly found the largest snake in the place. It could have wrapped its body twice around Uncle Vernonís car and crushed it into a trash canóbut at the moment it didnít look in the mood. In fact, it was fast asleep.

Dudley stood with his nose pressed against the glass, staring at the glistening brown coils.

ìMake it move,î he whined at his father. Uncle Vernon tapped on the glass, but the snake didnít budge.

ìDo it again,î Dudley ordered. Uncle Vernon rapped the glass smartly with his knuckles, but the snake just snoozed on.

ìThis is boring,î Dudley moaned. He shuffled away.

Harry moved in front of the tank and looked intently at the snake. He wouldnít have been surprised if it had died of boredom itselfóno company except stupid people drumming their fingers on the glass trying to disturb it all day long. It was worse than having a cupboard as a bedroom, where the only visitor was Aunt Petunia hammering on the door to wake you up; at least he got to visit the rest of the house.

The snake suddenly opened its beady eyes. Slowly, very slowly, it raised its head until its eyes were on a level with Harryís.

It winked.

Harry stared. Then he looked quickly around to see if anyone was watching. They werenít. He looked back at the snake and winked, too.

The snake jerked its head toward Uncle Vernon and Dudley, then raised its eyes to the ceiling. It gave Harry a look that said quite plainly:

ìI get that all the time.î

ìI know,î Harry murmured through the glass, though he wasnít sure the snake could hear him. ìIt must be really annoying.î

The snake nodded vigorously.

ìWhere do you come from, anyway?î Harry asked.

The snake jabbed its tail at a little sign next to the glass. Harry peered at it.



Boa Constrictor, Brazil.


ìWas it nice there?î

The boa constrictor jabbed its tail at the sign again and Harry read on: This specimen was bred in the zoo. ìOh, I seeóso youíve never been to Brazil?î

As the snake shook its head, a deafening shout behind Harry made both of them jump.

ìDUDLEY! MR. DURSLEY! COME AND LOOK AT THIS SNAKE! YOU WONíT BELIEVE WHAT ITíS DOING!î

Dudley came waddling toward them as fast as he could.

ìOut of the way, you,î he said, punching Harry in the ribs. Caught by surprise, Harry fell hard on the concrete floor. What came next happened so fast no one saw how it happenedóone second, Piers and Dudley were leaning right up close to the glass, the next, they had leapt back with howls of horror.

Harry sat up and gasped; the glass front of the boa constrictorís tank had vanished. The great snake was uncoiling itself rapidly, slithering out onto the floor. People throughout the reptile house screamed and started running for the exits.

As the snake slid swiftly past him, Harry could have sworn a low, hissing voice said, ìBrazil, here I comeÖ Thanksss, amigo.î

The keeper of the reptile house was in shock.

ìBut the glass,î he kept saying, ìwhere did the glass go?î

The zoo director himself made Aunt Petunia a cup of strong, sweet tea while he apologized over and over again. Piers and Dudley could only gibber. As far as Harry had seen, the snake hadnít done anything except snap playfully at their heels as it passed, but by the time they were all back in Uncle Vernonís car, Dudley was telling them how it had nearly bitten off his leg, while Piers was swearing it had tried to squeeze him to death. But worst of all, for Harry at least, was Piers calming down enough to say, ìHarry was talking to it, werenít you, Harry?î

Uncle Vernon waited until Piers was safely out of the house before starting on Harry. He was so angry he could hardly speak. He managed to say, ìGoócupboardóstayóno meals,î before he collapsed into a chair, and Aunt Petunia had to run and get him a large brandy.

Harry lay in his dark cupboard much later, wishing he had a watch. He didnít know what time it was and he couldnít be sure the Dursleys were asleep yet. Until they were, he couldnít risk sneaking to the kitchen for some food.

Heíd lived with the Dursleys almost ten years, ten miserable years, as long as he could remember, ever since heíd been a baby and his parents had died in that car crash. He couldnít remember being in the car when his parents had died. Sometimes, when he strained his memory during long hours in his cupboard, he came up with a strange vision: a blinding flash of green light and a burning pain on his forehead. This, he supposed, was the crash, though he couldnít imagine where all the green light came from. He couldnít remember his parents at all. His aunt and uncle never spoke about them, and of course he was forbidden to ask questions. There were no photographs of them in the house.

When he had been younger, Harry had dreamed and dreamed of some unknown relation coming to take him away, but it had never happened; the Dursleys were his only family. Yet sometimes he thought (or maybe hoped) that strangers in the street seemed to know him. Very strange strangers they were, too. A tiny man in a violet top hat had bowed to him once while out shopping with Aunt Petunia and Dudley. After asking Harry furiously if he knew the man, Aunt Petunia had rushed them out of the shop without buying anything. A wild looking old woman dressed all in green had waved merrily at him once on a bus. A bald man in a very long purple coat had actually shaken his hand in the street the other day and then walked away without a word. The weirdest thing about all these people was the way they seemed to vanish the second Harry tried to get a closer look.

At school, Harry had no one. Everybody knew that Dudleyís gang hated that odd Harry Potter in his baggy old clothes and broken glasses, and nobody liked to disagree with Dudleyís gang.




3. THE LETTERS FROM NO ONE


The escape of the Brazilian boa constrictor earned Harry his longest ever punishment. By the time he was allowed out of his cupboard again, the summer holidays had started and Dudley had already broken his new video camera, crashed his remote control airplane, and, first time out on his racing bike, knocked down old Mrs. Figg as she crossed Privet Drive on her crutches.

Harry was glad school was over, but there was no escaping Dudleyís gang, who visited the house every single day. Piers, Dennis, Malcolm, and Gordon were all big and stupid, but as Dudley was the biggest and stupidest of the lot, he was the leader. The rest of them were all quite happy to join in Dudleyís favorite sport: Harry Hunting.

This was why Harry spent as much time as possible out of the house, wandering around and thinking about the end of the holidays, where he could see a tiny ray of hope. When September came he would be going off to secondary school and, for the first time in his life, he wouldnít be with Dudley. Dudley had been accepted at Uncle Vernonís old private school, Smeltings. Piers Polkiss was going there too. Harry, on the other hand, was going to Stonewall High, the local public school. Dudley thought this was very funny.

ìThey stuff peopleís heads down the toilet the first day at Stonewall,î he told Harry. ìWant to come upstairs and practice?î

ìNo, thanks,î said Harry. ìThe poor toiletís never had anything as horrible as your head down itóit might be sick.î Then he ran, before Dudley could work out what heíd said.

One day in July, Aunt Petunia took Dudley to London to buy his Smeltings uniform, leaving Harry at Mrs. Figgís. Mrs. Figg wasnít as bad as usual. It turned out sheíd broken her leg tripping over one of her cats, and she didnít seem quite as fond of them as before. She let Harry watch television and gave him a bit of chocolate cake that tasted as though sheíd had it for several years.

That evening, Dudley paraded around the living room for the family in his brand new uniform. Smeltingsí boys wore maroon tailcoats, orange knickerbockers, and flat straw hats called boaters. They also carried knobbly sticks, used for hitting each other while the teachers werenít looking. This was supposed to be good training for later life.

As he looked at Dudley in his new knickerbockers, Uncle Vernon said gruffly that it was the proudest moment of his life. Aunt Petunia burst into tears and said she couldnít believe it was her Ickle Dudleykins, he looked so handsome and grown up. Harry didnít trust himself to speak. He thought two of his ribs might already have cracked from trying not to laugh.


* * *

There was a horrible smell in the kitchen the next morning when Harry went in for breakfast. It seemed to be coming from a large metal tub in the sink. He went to have a look. The tub was full of what looked like dirty rags swimming in gray water.

ìWhatís this?î he asked Aunt Petunia. Her lips tightened as they always did if he dared to ask a question.

ìYour new school uniform,î she said.

Harry looked in the bowl again.

ìOh,î he said, ìI didnít realize it had to be so wet.î

ìDonít be stupid,î snapped Aunt Petunia. ìIím dyeing some of Dudleyís old things gray for you. Itíll look just like everyone elseís when Iíve finished.î

Harry seriously doubted this, but thought it best not to argue. He sat down at the table and tried not to think about how he was going to look on his first day at Stonewall Highólike he was wearing bits of old elephant skin, probably.

Dudley and Uncle Vernon came in, both with wrinkled noses because of the smell from Harryís new uniform. Uncle Vernon opened his newspaper as usual and Dudley banged his Smelting stick, which he carried everywhere, on the table.

They heard the click of the mail slot and flop of letters on the doormat.

ìGet the mail, Dudley,î said Uncle Vernon from behind his paper.

ìMake Harry get it.î

ìGet the mail, Harry.î

ìMake Dudley get it.î

ìPoke him with your Smelting stick, Dudley.î

Harry dodged the Smelting stick and went to get the mail. Three things lay on the doormat: a postcard from Uncle Vernonís sister Marge, who was vacationing on the Isle of Wight, a brown envelope that looked like a bill, andóa letter for Harry. Harry picked it up and stared at it, his heart twanging like a giant elastic band. No one, ever, in his whole life, had written to him. Who would? He had no friends, no other relativesóhe didnít belong to the library, so heíd never even got rude notes asking for books back. Yet here it was, a letter, addressed so plainly there could be no mistake:



Mr. H. Potter

The Cupboard under the Stairs

4 Privet Drive

Little Whinging

Surrey


The envelope was thick and heavy, made of yellowish parchment, and the address was written in emerald green ink. There was no stamp.

Turning the envelope over, his hand trembling, Harry saw a purple wax seal bearing a coat of arms; a lion, an eagle, a badger, and a snake surrounding a large letter H.

ìHurry up, boy!î shouted Uncle Vernon from the kitchen. ìWhat are you doing, checking for letter bombs?î He chuckled at his own joke.

Harry went back to the kitchen, still staring at his letter. He handed Uncle Vernon the bill and the postcard, sat down, and slowly began to open the yellow envelope.

Uncle Vernon ripped open the bill, snorted in disgust, and flipped over the postcard.

ìMargeís ill,î he informed Aunt Petunia. ìAte a funny whelk.î

ìDad!î said Dudley suddenly. ìDad, Harryís got something!î

Harry was on the point of unfolding his letter, which was written on the same heavy parchment as the envelope, when it was jerked sharply out of his hand by Uncle Vernon.

ìThatís mine!î said Harry, trying to snatch it back.

ìWhoíd be writing to you?î sneered Uncle Vernon, shaking the letter open with one hand and glancing at it. His face went from red to green faster than a set of traffic lights. And it didnít stop there. Within seconds it was the grayish white of old porridge.

ìP-P-Petunia!î he gasped.

Dudley tried to grab the letter to read it, but Uncle Vernon held it high out of his reach. Aunt Petunia took it curiously and read the first line. For a moment it looked as though she might faint. She clutched her throat and made a choking noise.

ìVernon! Oh my goodnessóVernon!î

They stared at each other, seeming to have forgotten that Harry and Dudley were still in the room. Dudley wasnít used to being ignored. He gave his father a sharp tap on the head with his Smelting stick.

ìI want to read that letter,î he said loudly.

ìI want to read it,î said Harry furiously, ìas itís mine.î

ìGet out, both of you,î croaked Uncle Vernon, stuffing the letter back inside its envelope.

Harry didnít move.

ìI WANT MY LETTER!î he shouted.

ìLet me see it!î demanded Dudley.

ìOUT!î roared Uncle Vernon, and he took both Harry and Dudley by the scruffs of their necks and threw them into the hall, slamming the kitchen door behind them. Harry and Dudley promptly had a furious but silent fight over who would listen at the keyhole; Dudley won, so Harry, his glasses dangling from one ear, lay flat on his stomach to listen at the crack between door and floor.

ìVernon,î Aunt Petunia was saying in a quivering voice, ìlook at the addressóhow could they possibly know where he sleeps? You donít think theyíre watching the house?î

ìWatchingóspyingómight be following us,î muttered Uncle Vernon wildly.

ìBut what should we do, Vernon? Should we write back? Tell them we donít wantóî

Harry could see Uncle Vernonís shiny black shoes pacing up and down the kitchen.

ìNo,î he said finally. ìNo, weíll ignore it. If they donít get an answerÖ Yes, thatís bestÖ we wonít do anythingÖî

ìButóî

ìIím not having one in the house, Petunia! Didnít we swear when we took him in weíd stamp out that dangerous nonsense?î

That evening when he got back from work, Uncle Vernon did something heíd never done before; he visited Harry in his cupboard.

ìWhereís my letter?î said Harry, the moment Uncle Vernon had squeezed through the door. ìWhoís writing to me?î

ìNo one. it was addressed to you by mistake,î said Uncle Vernon shortly. ìI have burned it.î

ìIt was not a mistake,î said Harry angrily, ìit had my cupboard on it.î

ìSILENCE!î yelled Uncle Vernon, and a couple of spiders fell from the ceiling. He took a few deep breaths and then forced his face into a smile, which looked quite painful.

ìEróyes, Harryóabout this cupboard. Your aunt and I have been thinkingÖ youíre really getting a bit big for itÖ we think it might be nice if you moved into Dudleyís second bedroom.î

ìWhy?î said Harry.

ìDonít ask questions!î snapped his uncle. ìTake this stuff upstairs, now.î

The Dursleysí house had four bedrooms: one for Uncle Vernon and Aunt Petunia, one for visitors (usually Uncle Vernonís sister, Marge), one where Dudley slept, and one where Dudley kept all the toys and things that wouldnít fit into his first bedroom. It only took Harry one trip upstairs to move everything he owned from the cupboard to this room. He sat down on the bed and stared around him. Nearly everything in here was broken. The month old video camera was lying on top of a small, working tank Dudley had once driven over the next door neighborís dog; in the corner was Dudleyís first ever television set, which heíd put his foot through when his favorite program had been canceled; there was a large birdcage, which had once held a parrot that Dudley had swapped at school for a real air rifle, which was up on a shelf with the end all bent because Dudley had sat on it. Other shelves were full of books. They were the only things in the room that looked as though theyíd never been touched.

From downstairs came the sound of Dudley bawling at his mother, ìI donít want him in thereÖ I need that roomÖ make him get outÖî

Harry sighed and stretched out on the bed. Yesterday heíd have given anything to be up here. Today heíd rather be back in his cupboard with that letter than up here without it.

Next morning at breakfast, everyone was rather quiet. Dudley was in shock. Heíd screamed, whacked his father with his Smelting stick, been sick on purpose, kicked his mother, and thrown his tortoise through the greenhouse roof, and he still didnít have his room back. Harry was thinking about this time yesterday and bitterly wishing heíd opened the letter in the hall. Uncle Vernon and Aunt Petunia kept looking at each other darkly.

When the mail arrived, Uncle Vernon, who seemed to be trying to be nice to Harry, made Dudley go and get it. They heard him banging things with his Smelting stick all the way down the hall. Then he shouted, ìThereís another one! ëMr. H. Potter, The Smallest Bedroom, 4 Privet Driveóíî

With a strangled cry, Uncle Vernon leapt from his seat and ran down the hall, Harry right behind him. Uncle Vernon had to wrestle Dudley to the ground to get the letter from him, which was made difficult by the fact that Harry had grabbed Uncle Vernon around the neck from behind. After a minute of confused fighting, in which everyone got hit a lot by the Smelting stick, Uncle Vernon straightened up, gasping for breath, with Harryís letter clutched in his hand.

ìGo to your cupboardóI mean, your bedroom,î he wheezed at Harry. ìDudleyógoójust go.î

Harry walked round and round his new room. Someone knew he had moved out of his cupboard and they seemed to know he hadnít received his first letter. Surely that meant theyíd try again? And this time heíd make sure they didnít fail. He had a plan.

The repaired alarm clock rang at six oíclock the next morning. Harry turned it off quickly and dressed silently. He mustnít wake the Dursleys. He stole downstairs without turning on any of the lights.

He was going to wait for the postman on the corner of Privet Drive and get the letters for number four first. His heart hammered as he crept across the dark hall toward the front dooró

ìAAAAARRRGH!î

Harry leapt into the air; heíd trodden on something big and squashy on the doormatósomething alive!

Lights clicked on upstairs and to his horror Harry realized that the big, squashy something had been his uncleís face. Uncle Vernon had been lying at the foot of the front door in a sleeping bag, clearly making sure that Harry didnít do exactly what heíd been trying to do. He shouted at Harry for about half an hour and then told him to go and make a cup of tea. Harry shuffled miserably off into the kitchen and by the time he got back, the mail had arrived, right into Uncle Vernonís lap. Harry could see three letters addressed in green ink.

ìI wantóî he began, but Uncle Vernon was tearing the letters into pieces before his eyes.

Uncle Vernon didnt go to work that day. He stayed at home and nailed up the mail slot.

ìSee,î he explained to Aunt Petunia through a mouthful of nails, ìif they canít deliver them theyíll just give up.î

ìIím not sure thatíll work, Vernon.î

ìOh, these peopleís minds work in strange ways, Petunia, theyíre not like you and me,î said Uncle Vernon, trying to knock in a nail with the piece of fruitcake Aunt Petunia had just brought him.

On Friday, no less than twelve letters arrived for Harry. As they couldnít go through the mail slot they had been pushed under the door, slotted through the sides, and a few even forced through the small window in the downstairs bathroom.

Uncle Vernon stayed at home again. After burning all the letters, he got out a hammer and nails and boarded up the cracks around the front and back doors so no one could go out. He hummed ìTiptoe Through the Tulipsî as he worked, and jumped at small noises.

On Saturday, things began to get out of hand. Twenty four letters to Harry found their way into the house, rolled up and hidden inside each of the two dozen eggs that their very confused milkman had handed Aunt Petunia through the living room window. While Uncle Vernon made furious telephone calls to the post office and the dairy trying to find someone to complain to, Aunt Petunia shredded the letters in her food processor.

ìWho on earth wants to talk to you this badly?î Dudley asked Harry in amazement.


* * *

On Sunday morning, Uncle Vernon sat down at the breakfast table looking tired and rather ill, but happy.

ìNo post on Sundays,î he reminded them cheerfully as he spread marmalade on his newspapers, ìno damn letters todayóî

Something came whizzing down the kitchen chimney as he spoke and caught him sharply on the back of the head. Next moment, thirty or forty letters came pelting out of the fireplace like bullets. The Dursleys ducked, but Harry leapt into the air trying to catch oneó

ìOut! OUT!î

Uncle Vernon seized Harry around the waist and threw him into the hall. When Aunt Petunia and Dudley had run out with their arms over their faces, Uncle Vernon slammed the door shut. They could hear the letters still streaming into the room, bouncing off the walls and floor.

ìThat does it,î said Uncle Vernon, trying to speak calmly but pulling great tufts out of his mustache at the same time. ìI want you all back here in five minutes ready to leave. Weíre going away. Just pack some clothes. No arguments!î

He looked so dangerous with half his mustache missing that no one dared argue. Ten minutes later they had wrenched their way through the boarded up doors and were in the car, speeding toward the highway. Dudley was sniffling in the back seat; his father had hit him round the head for holding them up while he tried to pack his television, VCR, and computer in his sports bag.

They drove. And they drove. Even Aunt Petunia didnít dare ask where they were going. Every now and then Uncle Vernon would take a sharp turn and drive in the opposite direction for a while.

ìShake íem offÖ shake íem off,î he would mutter whenever he did this.

They didnít stop to eat or drink all day. By nightfall Dudley was howling. Heíd never had such a bad day in his life. He was hungry, heíd missed five television programs heíd wanted to see, and heíd never gone so long without blowing up an alien on his computer.

Uncle Vernon stopped at last outside a gloomy looking hotel on the outskirts of a big city. Dudley and Harry shared a room with twin beds and damp, musty sheets. Dudley snored but Harry stayed awake, sitting on the windowsill, staring down at the lights of passing cars and wonderingÖ

They ate stale cornflakes and cold tinned tomatoes on toast for breakfast the next day. They had just finished when the owner of the hotel came over to their table.

ìíScuse me, but is one of you Mr. H. Potter? Only I got about an íundred of these at the front desk.î

She held up a letter so they could read the green ink address:



Mr. H. Potter

Room 17

Railview Hotel

Cokeworth


Harry made a grab for the letter but Uncle Vernon knocked his hand out of the way. The woman stared.

ìIíll take them,î said Uncle Vernon, standing up quickly and following her from the dining room.


* * *

ìWouldnít it be better just to go home, dear?î Aunt Petunia suggested timidly, hours later, but Uncle Vernon didnít seem to hear her. Exactly what he was looking for, none of them knew. He drove them into the middle of a forest, got out, looked around, shook his head, got back in the car, and off they went again. The same thing happened in the middle of a plowed field, halfway across a suspension bridge, and at the top of a multilevel parking garage.

ìDaddyís gone mad, hasnít he?î Dudley asked Aunt Petunia dully late that afternoon. Uncle Vernon had parked at the coast, locked them all inside the car, and disappeared.

It started to rain. Great drops beat on the roof of the car. Dud ley sniveled.

ìItís Monday,î he told his mother. ìThe Great Humbertoís on tonight. I want to stay somewhere with a television.î

Monday. This reminded Harry of something. If it was Mondayóand you could usually count on Dudley to know the days the week, because of televisionóthen tomorrow, Tuesday, was Harryís eleventh birthday. Of course, his birthdays were never exactly funólast year, the Dursleys had given him a coat hanger and a pair of Uncle Vernonís old socks. Still, you werenít eleven every day.

Uncle Vernon was back and he was smiling. He was also carrying a long, thin package and didnít answer Aunt Petunia when she asked what heíd bought.

ìFound the perfect place!î he said. ìCome on! Everyone out!î

It was very cold outside the car. Uncle Vernon was pointing at what looked like a large rock way out at sea. Perched on top of the rock was the most miserable little shack you could imagine. One thing was certain, there was no television in there.

ìStorm forecast for tonight!î said Uncle Vernon gleefully, clapping his hands together. ìAnd this gentlemanís kindly agreed to lend us his boat!î

A toothless old man came ambling up to them, pointing, with a rather wicked grin, at an old rowboat bobbing in the iron gray water below them.

ìIíve already got us some rations,î said Uncle Vernon, ìso all aboard!î

It was freezing in the boat. Icy sea spray and rain crept down their necks and a chilly wind whipped their faces. After what seemed like hours they reached the rock, where Uncle Vernon, slipping and sliding, led the way to the broken down house.

The inside was horrible; it smelled strongly of seaweed, the wind whistled through the gaps in the wooden walls, and the fireplace was damp and empty. There were only two rooms.

Uncle Vernonís rations turned out to be a bag of chips each and four bananas. He tried to start a fire but the empty chip bags just smoked and shriveled up.

ìCould do with some of those letters now, eh?î he said cheerfully.

He was in a very good mood. Obviously he thought nobody stood a chance of reaching them here in a storm to deliver mail. Harry privately agreed, though the thought didnít cheer him up at all.

As night fell, the promised storm blew up around them. Spray from the high waves splattered the walls of the hut and a fierce wind rattled the filthy windows. Aunt Petunia found a few moldy blankets in the second room and made up a bed for Dudley on the moth eaten sofa. She and Uncle Vernon went off to the lumpy bed next door, and Harry was left to find the softest bit of floor he could and to curl up under the thinnest, most ragged blanket.

The storm raged more and more ferociously as the night went on. Harry couldnít sleep. He shivered and turned over, trying to get comfortable, his stomach rumbling with hunger. Dudleyís snores were drowned by the low rolls of thunder that started near midnight. The lighted dial of Dudleyís watch, which was dangling over the edge of the sofa on his fat wrist, told Harry heíd be eleven in ten minutesí time. He lay and watched his birthday tick nearer, wondering if the Dursleys would remember at all, wondering where the letter writer was now.

Five minutes to go. Harry heard something creak outside. He hoped the roof wasnít going to fall in, although he might be warmer if it did. Four minutes to go. Maybe the house in Privet Drive would be so full of letters when they got back that heíd be able to steal one somehow.

Three minutes to go. Was that the sea, slapping hard on the rock like that? And (two minutes to go) what was that funny crunching noise? Was the rock crumbling into the sea?

One minute to go and heíd be eleven. Thirty secondsÖ twentyÖ tenÖ nineómaybe heíd wake Dudley up, just to annoy himóthreeÖ twoÖ oneÖ

BOOM.

The whole shack shivered and Harry sat bolt upright, staring at the door. Someone was outside, knocking to come in.




4. THE KEEPER OF THE KEYS


BOOM. They knocked again. Dudley jerked awake. ìWhereís the cannon?î he said stupidly.

There was a crash behind them and Uncle Vernon came skidding into the room. He was holding a rifle in his handsónow they knew what had been in the long, thin package he had brought with them.

ìWhoís there?î he shouted. ìI warn youóIím armed!î

There was a pause. Thenó

SMASH!

The door was hit with such force that it swung clean off its hinges and with a deafening crash landed flat on the floor.

A giant of a man was standing in the doorway. His face was almost completely hidden by a long, shaggy mane of hair and a wild, tangled beard, but you could make out his eyes, glinting like black beetles under all the hair.

The giant squeezed his way into the hut, stooping so that his head just brushed the ceiling. He bent down, picked up the door, and fitted it easily back into its frame. The noise of the storm outside dropped a little. He turned to look at them all.

ìCouldnít make us a cup oí tea, could yeh? Itís not been an easy journeyÖî

He strode over to the sofa where Dudley sat frozen with fear.

ìBudge up, yeh great lump,î said the stranger.

Dudley squeaked and ran to hide behind his mother, who was crouching, terrified, behind Uncle Vernon.

ìAní hereís Harry!î said the giant.

Harry looked up into the fierce, wild, shadowy face and saw that the beetle eyes were crinkled in a smile.

ìLasí time I saw you, you was only a baby,î said the giant. ìYeh look a lot like yet dad, but yehíve got yet momís eyes.î

Uncle Vernon made a funny rasping noise.

ìI demand that you leave at once, sir!î he said. ìYou are breaking and entering!î

ìAh, shut up, Dursley, yeh great prune,î said the giant; he reached over the back of the sofa, jerked the gun out of Uncle Vernonís hands, bent it into a knot as easily as if it had been made of rubber, and threw it into a corner of the room.

Uncle Vernon made another funny noise, like a mouse being trodden on.

ìAnywayóHarry,î said the giant, turning his back on the Dursleys, ìa very happy birthday to yeh. Got summat fer yeh hereóI mighta sat on it at some point, but itíll taste all right.î

From an inside pocket of his black overcoat he pulled a slightly squashed box. Harry opened it with trembling fingers. Inside was a large, sticky chocolate cake with Happy Birthday Harry written on it in green icing.

Harry looked up at the giant. He meant to say thank you, but the words got lost on the way to his mouth, and what he said instead was, ìWho are you?î

The giant chuckled.

ìTrue, I havenít introduced meself. Rubeus Hagrid, Keeper of Keys and Grounds at Hogwarts.î

He held out an enormous hand and shook Harryís whole arm.

ìWhat about that tea then, eh?î he said, rubbing his hands together. ìIíd not say no ter summat stronger if yehíve got it, mind.î

His eyes fell on the empty grate with the shriveled chip bags in it and he snorted. He bent down over the fireplace; they couldnít see what he was doing but when he drew back a second later, there was a roaring fire there. It filled the whole damp hut with flickering light and Harry felt the warmth wash over him as though heíd sunk into a hot bath.

The giant sat back down on the sofa, which sagged under his weight, and began taking all sorts of things out of the pockets of his coat: a copper kettle, a squashy package of sausages, a poker, a teapot, several chipped mugs, and a bottle of some amber liquid that he took a swig from before starting to make tea. Soon the hut was full of the sound and smell of sizzling sausage. Nobody said a thing while the giant was working, but as he slid the first six fat, juicy, slightly burnt sausages from the poker, Dudley fidgeted a little. Uncle Vernon said sharply, ìDonít touch anything he gives you, Dudley.î

The giant chuckled darkly.

ìYer great puddiní of a son doní need fatteniní anymore, Dursley, doní worry.î

He passed the sausages to Harry, who was so hungry he had never tasted anything so wonderful, but he still couldnít take his eyes off the giant. Finally, as nobody seemed about to explain anything, he said, ìIím sorry, but I still donít really know who you are.î

The giant took a gulp of tea and wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.

ìCall me Hagrid,î he said, ìeveryone does. Aní like I told yeh, Iím Keeper of Keys at Hogwartsóyehíll know all about Hogwarts, oí course.î

ìEróno,î said Harry.

Hagrid looked shocked.

ìSorry,î Harry said quickly.

ìSorry?î barked Hagrid, turning to stare at the Dursleys, who shrank back into the shadows. ìItí s them as should be sorry! I knew yeh werenít gettiní yer letters but I never thought yeh wouldnít even know abouí Hogwarts, fer cryiní out loud! Did yeh never wonder where yet parents learned it all?î

ìAll what?î asked Harry.

ìALL WHAT?î Hagrid thundered. ìNow wait jusí one second!î

He had leapt to his feet. In his anger he seemed to fill the whole hut. The Dursleys were cowering against the wall.

ìDo you mean ter tell me,î he growled at the Dursleys, ìthat this boyóthis boy!óknows nothiní abouíóabout ANYTHING?î

Harry thought this was going a bit far. He had been to school, after all, and his marks werenít bad.

ìI know some things,î he said. ìI can, you know, do math and stuff.î But Hagrid simply waved his hand and said, ìAbout our world, I mean. Your world. My world. Yer parentsí world.î

ìWhat world?î

Hagrid looked as if he was about to explode.

ìDURSLEY!î he boomed.

Uncle Vernon, who had gone very pale, whispered something that sounded like ìMimblewimble.î Hagrid stared wildly at Harry.

ìBut yeh must know about yet mom and dad,î he said. ìI mean, theyíre famous. Youíre famous.î

ìWhat? Myómy mom and dad werenít famous, were they?î

ìYeh doní knowÖ yeh doní knowÖî Hagrid ran his fingers through his hair, fixing Harry with a bewildered stare.

ìYeh doní know what yeh are?î he said finally.

Uncle Vernon suddenly found his voice.

ìStop!î he commanded. ìStop right there, sit! I forbid you to tell the boy anything!î

A braver man than Vernon Dursley would have quailed under the furious look Hagrid now gave him; when Hagrid spoke, his every syllable trembled with rage.

ìYou never told him? Never told him what was in the letter Dumbledore left fer him? I was there! I saw Dumbledore leave it, Dursley! Aní youíve kept it from him all these years?î

ìKept what from me?î said Harry eagerly.

ìSTOP! I FORBID YOU!î yelled Uncle Vernon in panic.

Aunt Petunia gave a gasp of horror.

ìAh, go boil yet heads, both of yeh,î said Hagrid. ìHarryóyer a wizard.î

There was silence inside the hut. Only the sea and the whistling wind could be heard.

ìIím a what?î gasped Harry.

ìA wizard, oí course,î said Hagrid, sitting back down on the sofa, which groaned and sank even lower, ìaní a thumpiní goodíun, Iíd say, once yehíve been trained up a bit. With a mum aní dad like yours, what else would yeh be? Aní I reckon itís abouí time yeh read yer letter.î

Harry stretched out his hand at last to take the yellowish envelope, addressed in emerald green to Mr. H. Potter, The Floor, Hut on the Rock, The Sea. He pulled out the letter and read:



HOGWARTS SCHOOL of WITCHCRAFT and WIZARDRY

Headmaster: ALBUS DUMBLEDORE

(Order of Merlin, First Class, Grand Sorc., Chf. Warlock, Supreme Mugwump, International Confed. of Wizards)



Dear Mr. Potter,

We are pleased to inform you that you have been accepted at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. Please find enclosed a list of all necessary books and equipment.

Term begins on September 1. We await your owl by no later than July 31.

Yours sincerely,

Minerva McGonagall,

Deputy Headmistress


Questions exploded inside Harryís head like fireworks and he couldnít decide which to ask first. After a few minutes he stammered, ìWhat does it mean, they await my owl?î

ìGallopiní Gorgons, that reminds me,î said Hagrid, clapping a hand to his forehead with enough force to knock over a cart horse, and from yet another pocket inside his overcoat he pulled an owlóa real, live, rather ruffled looking owlóa long quill, and a roll of parchment. With his tongue between his teeth he scribbled a note that Harry could read upside down:



Dear Professor Dumbledore,

Given Harry his letter.

Taking him to buy his things tomorrow.

Weatherís horrible. Hope youíre well.

Hagrid


Hagrid rolled up the note, gave it to the owl, which clamped it in its beak, went to the door, and threw the owl out into the storm. Then he came back and sat down as though this was as normal as talking on the telephone.

Harry realized his mouth was open and closed it quickly.

ìWhere was I?î said Hagrid, but at that moment, Uncle Vernon, still ashen faced but looking very angry, moved into the firelight.

ìHeís not going,î he said.

Hagrid grunted.

ìIíd like ter see a great Muggle like you stop him,î he said.

ìA what?î said Harry, interested.

ìA Muggle,î said Hagrid, ìitís what we call nonmagic folk like them. Aní itís your bad luck you grew up in a family oí the biggest Muggles I ever laid eyes on.î

ìWe swore when we took him in weíd put a stop to that rubbish,î said Uncle Vernon, ìswore weíd stamp it out of him! Wizard indeed!î

ìYou knew?î said Harry. ìYou knew Iím aóa wizard?î

ìKnew!î shrieked Aunt Petunia suddenly. ìKnew! Of course we knew! How could you not be, my dratted sister being what she was? Oh, she got a letter just like that and disappeared off to thatóthat schoolóand came home every vacation with her pockets full of frog spawn, turning teacups into rats. I was the only one who saw her for what she wasóa freak! But for my mother and father, oh no, it was Lily this and Lily that, they were proud of having a witch in the family!î

She stopped to draw a deep breath and then went ranting on. It seemed she had been wanting to say all this for years.

ìThen she met that Potter at school and they left and got married and had you, and of course I knew youíd be just the same, just as strange, just asóasóabnormalóand then, if you please, she went and got herself blown up and we got landed with you!î

Harry had gone very white. As soon as he found his voice he said, ìBlown up? You told me they died in a car crash!î

ìCAR CRASH!î roared Hagrid, jumping up so angrily that the Dursleys scuttled back to their corner. ìHow could a car crash kill Lily aní James Potter? Itís an outrage! A scandal! Harry Potter not knowiní his own story when every kid in our world knows his name!î

ìBut why? What happened?î Harry asked urgently.

The anger faded from Hagridís face. He looked suddenly anxious.

ìI never expected this,î he said, in a low, worried voice. ìI had no idea, when Dumbledore told me there might be trouble gettiní hold of yeh, how much yeh didnít know. Ah, Harry, I doní know if Iím the right person ter tell yehóbut someoneís gottaóyeh canít go off ter Hogwarts not knowiní.î

He threw a dirty look at the Dursleys.

ìWell, itís best yeh know as much as I can tell yehómind, I canít tell yeh everythiní, itís a great mystíry, parts of itÖî

He sat down, stared into the fire for a few seconds, and then said, ìIt begins, I suppose, withówith a person calledóbut itís incredible yeh donít know his name, everyone in our world knowsóî

ìWho?î

ìWellóI doní like sayiní the name if I can help it. No one does.î

ìWhy not?î

ìGulpiní gargoyles, Harry, people are still scared. Blimey, this is difficult. See, there was this wizard who wentÖ bad. As bad as you could go. Worse. Worse than worse. His name wasÖî

Hagrid gulped, but no words came out.

ìCould you write it down?î Harry suggested.

ìNah canít spell it. All rightóVoldemort.î Hagrid shuddered. ìDonít make me say it again. Anyway, thisóthis wizard, about twenty years ago now, started lookiní fer followers. Got íem, tooósome were afraid, some just wanted a bit oí his power, ícause he was gettiní himself power, all right. Dark days, Harry. Didnít know who ter trust, didnít dare get friendly with strange wizards or witchesÖ terrible things happened. He was takiní over. íCourse, some stood up to himóaní he killed íem. Horribly. One oí the only safe places left was Hogwarts. Reckon Dumbledoreís the only one You-Know-Who was afraid of. Didnít dare try takiní the school, not jusí then, anyway.

ìNow, yer mum aní dad were as good a witch aní wizard as I ever knew. Head boy aní girl at Hogwarts in their day! Suppose the mystíry is why You-Know-Who never tried to get íem on his side beforeÖ probably knew they were too close ter Dumbledore ter want anythiní ter do with the Dark Side.

ìMaybe he thought he could persuade íemÖ maybe he just wanted íem outta the way. All anyone knows is, he turned up in the village where you was all living, on Halloween ten years ago. You was just a year old. He came ter yer house aníóaníóî

Hagrid suddenly pulled out a very dirty, spotted handkerchief and blew his nose with a sound like a foghorn.

ìSorry,î he said. ìBut itís that sadóknew yer mum aní dad, aní nicer people yeh couldnít findóanywayÖî

ìYou-Know-Who killed íem. Aní thenóaní this is the real mystíry of the thingóhe tried to kill you, too. Wanted ter make a clean job of it, I suppose, or maybe he just liked killiní by then. But he couldnít do it. Never wondered how you got that mark on yer forehead? That was no ordinary cut.
