Thatís what yeh get when a powerful, evil curse touches yehótook care of yer mum aní dad aní yer house, evenóbut it didnít work on you, aní thatís why yer famous, Harry. No one ever lived after he decided ter kill ëem, no one except you, aní heíd killed some oí the best witches aní wizards of the ageóthe McKinnons, the Bones, the Prewettsóaní you was only a baby, aní you lived.î

Something very painful was going on in Harryís mind. As Hagridís story came to a close, he saw again the blinding flash of green light, more clearly than he had ever remembered it beforeóand he remembered something else, for the first time in his life: a high, cold, cruel laugh.

Hagrid was watching him sadly.

ìTook yeh from the ruined house myself, on Dumbledoreís orders. Brought yeh ter this lotÖî

ìLoad of old tosh,î said Uncle Vernon. Harry jumped; he had almost forgotten that the Dursleys were there. Uncle Vernon certainly seemed to have got back his courage. He was glaring at Hagrid and his fists were clenched.

ìNow, you listen here, boy,î he snarled, ìI accept thereís something strange about you, probably nothing a good beating wouldnít have curedóand as for all this about your parents, well, they were weirdos, no denying it, and the worldís better off without them in my opinionóasked for all they got, getting mixed up with these wizarding typesójust what I expected, always knew theyíd come to a sticky endóî

But at that moment, Hagrid leapt from the sofa and drew a battered pink umbrella from inside his coat. Pointing this at Uncle Vernon like a sword, he said, ìIím warning you, DursleyóIím warning youóone more wordÖî

In danger of being speared on the end of an umbrella by a bearded giant, Uncle Vernonís courage failed again; he flattened himself against the wall and fell silent.

ìThatís better,î said Hagrid, breathing heavily and sitting back down on the sofa, which this time sagged right down to the floor.

Harry, meanwhile, still had questions to ask, hundreds of them.

ìBut what happened to Voló, sorryóI mean, You-Know-Who?î

ìGood question, Harry. Disappeared. Vanished. Same night he tried ter kill you. Makes yeh even more famous. Thatís the biggest mystíry, seeÖ he was gettiní more aní more powerfulówhyíd he go?

ìSome say he died. Codswallop, in my opinion. Dunno if he had enough human left in him to die. Some say heís still out there, bidiní his time, like, but I doní believe it. People who was on his side came back ter ours. Some of íem came outta kinda trances. Doní reckon they couldíve done if he was cominí back.

ìMost of us reckon heís still out there somewhere but lost his powers. Too weak to carry on. íCause somethiní about you finished him, Harry. There was somethiní goiní on that night he hadnít counted onóI dunno what it was, no one doesóbut somethiní about you stumped him, all right.î

Hagrid looked at Harry with warmth and respect blazing in his eyes, but Harry, instead of feeling pleased and proud, felt quite sure there had been a horrible mistake. A wizard? Him? How could he possibly be? Heíd spent his life being clouted by Dudley, and bullied by Aunt Petunia and Uncle Vernon; if he was really a wizard, why hadnít they been turned into warty toads every time theyíd tried to lock him in his cupboard? If heíd once defeated the greatest sorcerer in the world, how come Dudley had always been able to kick him around like a football?

ìHagrid,î he said quietly, ìI think you must have made a mistake. I donít think I can be a wizard.î

To his surprise, Hagrid chuckled.

ìNot a wizard, eh? Never made things happen when you was scared or angry?î

Harry looked into the fire. Now he came to think about itÖ every odd thing that had ever made his aunt and uncle furious with him had happened when he, Harry, had been upset or angryÖ chased by Dudleyís gang, he had somehow found himself out of their reachÖ dreading going to school with that ridiculous haircut, heíd managed to make it grow backÖ and the very last time Dudley had hit him, hadnít he got his revenge, without even realizing he was doing it? Hadnít he set a boa constrictor on him?

Harry looked back at Hagrid, smiling, and saw that Hagrid was positively beaming at him.

ìSee?î said Hagrid. ìHarry Potter, not a wizardóyou wait, youíll be right famous at Hogwarts.î

But Uncle Vernon wasnít going to give in without a fight.

ìHavenít I told you heís not going?î he hissed. ìHeís going to Stonewall High and heíll be grateful for it. Iíve read those letters and he needs all sorts of rubbishóspell books and wands andóî

ìIf he wants ter go, a great Muggle like you wonít stop him,î growled Hagrid. ìStop Lily aní James Potterís son goiní ter Hogwarts! Yer mad. His nameís been down ever since he was born. Heís off ter the finest school of witchcraft and wizardry in the world. Seven years there and he wonít know himself. Heíll be with youngsters of his own sort, fer a change, aní heíll be under the greatest headmaster Hogwarts ever had Albus Dumbledóî

ìI AM NOT PAYING FOR SOME CRACKPOT OLD FOOL To TEACH HIM MAGIC TRICKS!î yelled Uncle Vernon.

But he had finally gone too far. Hagrid seized his umbrella and whirled it over his head, ìNEVER,î he thundered, ìóINSULTóALBUSóDUMBLEDOREóINóFRONTóOFóME!î

He brought the umbrella swishing down through the air to point at Dudleyóthere was a flash of violet light, a sound like a firecracker, a sharp squeal, and the next second, Dudley was dancing on the spot with his hands clasped over his fat bottom, howling in pain. When he turned his back on them, Harry saw a curly pigís tail poking through a hole in his trousers.

Uncle Vernon roared. Pulling Aunt Petunia and Dudley into the other room, he cast one last terrified look at Hagrid and slammed the door behind them.

Hagrid looked down at his umbrella and stroked his beard.

ìShouldníta lost me temper,î he said ruefully, ìbut it didnít work anyway. Meant ter turn him into a pig, but I suppose he was so much like a pig anyway there wasnít much left ter do.î

He cast a sideways look at Harry under his bushy eyebrows.

ìBe grateful if yeh didnít mention that ter anyone at Hogwarts,î he said. ìIímóerónot supposed ter do magic, strictly speakiní. I was allowed ter do a bit ter follow yeh aní get yer letters to yeh aní stuffóone oí the reasons I was so keen ter take on the jobóî

ìWhy arenít you supposed to do magic?î asked Harry.

ìOh, wellóI was at Hogwarts meself but Ióerógot expelled, ter tell yeh the truth. In me third year. They snapped me wand in half aní everything. But Dumbledore let me stay on as gamekeeper. Great man, Dumbledore.î

ìWhy were you expelled?î

ìItís gettiní late and weíve got lots ter do tomorrow,î said Hagrid loudly. ìGotta get up ter town, get all yer books aní that.î

He took off his thick black coat and threw it to Harry.

ìYou can kip under that,î he said. ìDoní mind if it wriggles a bit, I think I still got a couple oí dormice in one oí the pockets.î




5. DIAGON ALLEY


Harry woke early the next morning. Although he could tell it was daylight, he kept his eyes shut tight.

ìIt was a dream, he told himself firmly. ìI dreamed a giant called Hagrid came to tell me I was going to a school for wizards. When I open my eyes Iíll be at home in my cupboard.î

There was suddenly a loud tapping noise.

And thereís Aunt Petunia knocking on the door, Harry thought, his heart sinking. But he still didnít open his eyes. It had been such a good dream.

Tap. Tap. Tap.

ìAll right,î Harry mumbled, ìIím getting up.î

He sat up and Hagridís heavy coat fell off him. The hut was full of sunlight, the storm was over, Hagrid himself was asleep on the collapsed sofa, and there was an owl rapping its claw on the window, a newspaper held in its beak.

Harry scrambled to his feet, so happy he felt as though a large balloon was swelling inside him. He went straight to the window and jerked it open. The owl swooped in and dropped the newspaper on top of Hagrid, who didnít wake up. The owl then fluttered onto the floor and began to attack Hagridís coat.

ìDonít do that.î

Harry tried to wave the owl out of the way, but it snapped its beak fiercely at him and carried on savaging the coat.

ìHagrid!î said Harry loudly. ìThereís an owlóî

ìPay him,î Hagrid grunted into the sofa.

ìWhat?î

ìHe wants payiní fer deliveriní the paper. Look in the pockets.î

Hagridís coat seemed to be made of nothing but pocketsóbunches of keys, slug pellets, balls of string, peppermint humbugs, teabagsÖ finally, Harry pulled out a handful of strange looking coins.

ìGive him five Knuts,î said Hagrid sleepily.

ìKnuts?î

ìThe little bronze ones.î

Harry counted out five little bronze coins, and the owl held out his leg so Harry could put the money into a small leather pouch tied to it. Then he flew off through the open window.

Hagrid yawned loudly, sat up, and stretched.

ìBest be off, Harry, lots ter do today, gotta get up ter London aní buy all yer stuff fer school.î

Harry was turning over the wizard coins and looking at them. He had just thought of something that made him feel as though the happy balloon inside him had got a puncture.

ìUmóHagrid?î

ìMm?î said Hagrid, who was pulling on his huge boots.

ìI havenít got any moneyóand you heard Uncle Vernon last nightÖ he wonít pay for me to go and learn magic.î

ìDonít worry about that,î said Hagrid, standing up and scratching his head. ìDíyeh think yer parents didnít leave yeh anything?î

ìBut if their house was destroyedóî

ìThey didní keep their gold in the house, boy! Nah, first stop fer us is Gringotts. Wizardsí bank. Have a sausage, theyíre not bad coldóaní I wouldní say no teh a bit oí yer birthday cake, neither.î

ìWizards have banks?î

ìJust the one. Gringotts. Run by goblins.î

Harry dropped the bit of sausage he was holding.

ìGoblins?î

ìYeahóso yehíd be mad ter try aní rob it, Iíll tell yeh that. Never mess with goblins, Harry. Gringotts is the safest place in the world fer anything yeh want ter keep safeóícept maybe Hogwarts. As a matter oí fact, I gotta visit Gringotts anyway. Fer Dumbledore. Hogwarts business.î Hagrid drew himself up proudly. ìHe usually gets me ter do important stuff fer him. Fetchiní you gettiní things from Gringottsóknows he can trust me, see.

ìGot everythiní? Come on, then.î

Harry followed Hagrid out onto the rock. The sky was quite clear now and the sea gleamed in the sunlight. The boat Uncle Vernon had hired was still there, with a lot of water in the bottom after the storm.

ìHow did you get here?î Harry asked, looking around for another boat.

ìFlew,î said Hagrid.

ìFlew?î

ìYeahóbut weíll go back in this. Not sípposed ter use magic now Iíve got yeh.î

They settled down in the boat, Harry still staring at Hagrid, trying to imagine him flying.

ìSeems a shame ter row, though,î said Hagrid, giving Harry another of his sideways looks. ìIf I was teróeróspeed things up a bit, would yeh mind not mentioniní it at Hogwarts?î

ìOf course not,î said Harry, eager to see more magic. Hagrid pulled out the pink umbrella again, tapped it twice on the side of the boat, and they sped off toward land.

ìWhy would you be mad to try and rob Gringotts?î Harry asked.

ìSpellsóenchantments,î said Hagrid, unfolding his newspaper as he spoke. ìThey say thereís dragons guardiní the highsecurity vaults. And then yeh gotta find yer wayóGringotts is hundreds of miles under London, see. Deep under the Underground. Yehíd die of hunger tryiní ter get out, even if yeh did manage ter get yer hands on summat.î

Harry sat and thought about this while Hagrid read his newspaper, the Daily Prophet. Harry had learned from Uncle Vernon that people liked to be left alone while they did this, but it was very difficult, heíd never had so many questions in his life.

ìMinistry oí Magic messiní things up as usual,î Hagrid muttered, turning the page.

ìThereís a Ministry of Magic?î Harry asked, before he could stop himself.

ìíCourse,î said Hagrid. ìThey wanted Dumbledore fer Minister, oí course, but heíd never leave Hogwarts, so old Cornelius Fudge got the job. Bungler if ever there was one. So he pelts Dumbledore with owls every morning, askiní fer advice.î

ìBut what does a Ministry of Magic do?î

ìWell, their main job is to keep it from the Muggles that thereís still witches aní wizards up aní down the country.î

ìWhy?î

ìWhy? Blimey, Harry, everyoneíd be wantiní magic solutions to their problems. Nah, weíre best left alone.î

At this moment the boat bumped gently into the harbor wall. Hagrid folded up his newspaper, and they clambered up the stone steps onto the street.

Passersby stared a lot at Hagrid as they walked through the little town to the station. Harry couldnít blame them. Not only was Hagrid twice as tall as anyone else, he kept pointing at perfectly ordinary things like parking meters and saying loudly, ìSee that, Harry? Things these Muggles dream up, eh?î

ìHagrid,î said Harry, panting a bit as he ran to keep up, ìdid you say there are dragons at Gringotts?î

ìWell, so they say,î said Hagrid. ìCrikey, Iíd like a dragon.î

ìYouíd like one?î

ìWanted one ever since I was a kidóhere we go.î

They had reached the station. There was a train to London in five minutesí time. Hagrid, who didnít understand ìMuggle money,î as he called it, gave the bills to Harry so he could buy their tickets.

People stared more than ever on the train. Hagrid took up two seats and sat knitting what looked like a canary yellow circus tent.

ìStill got yer letter, Harry?î he asked as he counted stitches. Harry took the parchment envelope out of his pocket.

ìGood,î said Hagrid. ìThereís a list there of everything yeh need.î

Harry unfolded a second piece of paper he hadnít noticed the night before, and read:



HOGWARTS SCHOOL of WITCHCRAFT and WIZARDRY

UNIFORM

First year students will require:

1. Three sets of plain work robes (black)

2. One plain pointed hat (black) for day wear

3. One pair of protective gloves (dragon hide or similar)

4. One winter cloak (black, silver fastenings)

Please note that all pupilsí clothes should carry name tags

COURSE BOOKS

All students should have a copy of each of the following:

The Standard Book of Spells (Grade 1) by Miranda Goshawk

A History of Magic by Bathilda Bagshot

Magical Theory by Adalbert Waffling

A Beginnersí Guide to Transfiguration by Emetic Switch

One Thousand Magical Herbs and Fungi by Phyllida Spore

Magical Drafts and Potions by Arsenius Jigger

Fantastic Beasts and Where to Find Them by Newt Scamander

The Dark Forces: A Guide to Self Protection by Quentin Trimble

OTHER EQUIPMENT

1 wand

1 cauldron (pewter, standard size 2) set

1 set of glass or crystal phials

1 telescope set

1 brass scales

Students may also bring an owl OR a cat OR a toad

PARENTS ARE REMINDED THAT FIRST YEARS ARE NOT ALLOWED THEIR OWN BROOMSTICKS


ìCan we buy all this in London?î Harry wondered aloud.

ìIf yeh know where to go,î said Hagrid.

Harry had never been to London before. Although Hagrid seemed to know where he was going, he was obviously not used to getting there in an ordinary way. He got stuck in the ticket barrier on the Underground, and complained loudly that the seats were too small and the trains too slow.

ìI donít know how the Muggles manage without magic,î he said as they climbed a broken down escalator that led up to a bustling road lined with shops.

Hagrid was so huge that he parted the crowd easily; all Harry had to do was keep close behind him. They passed book shops and music stores, hamburger restaurants and cinemas, but nowhere that looked as if it could sell you a magic wand. This was just an ordinary street full of ordinary people. Could there really be piles of wizard gold buried miles beneath them? Were there really shops that sold spell books and broomsticks? Might this not all be some huge joke that the Dursleys had cooked up? If Harry hadnít known that the Dursleys had no sense of humor, he might have thought so; yet somehow, even though everything Hagrid had told him so far was unbelievable, Harry couldnít help trusting him.

ìThis is it,î said Hagrid, coming to a halt, ìthe Leaky Cauldron. Itís a famous place.î

It was a tiny, grubby looking pub. If Hagrid hadnít pointed it out, Harry wouldnít have noticed it was there. The people hurrying by didnít glance at it. Their eyes slid from the big book shop on one side to the record shop on the other as if they couldnít see the Leaky Cauldron at all. In fact, Harry had the most peculiar feeling that only he and Hagrid could see it. Before he could mention this, Hagrid had steered him inside.

For a famous place, it was very dark and shabby. A few old women were sitting in a corner, drinking tiny glasses of sherry. One of them was smoking a long pipe. A little man in a top hat was talking to the old bartender, who was quite bald and looked like a toothless walnut. The low buzz of chatter stopped when they walked in. Everyone seemed to know Hagrid; they waved and smiled at him, and the bartender reached for a glass, saying, ìThe usual, Hagrid?î

ìCanít, Tom, Iím on Hogwarts business,î said Hagrid, clapping his great hand on Harryís shoulder and making Harryís knees buckle.

ìGood Lord,î said the bartender, peering at Harry, ìis thisócan this be ?î

The Leaky Cauldron had suddenly gone completely still and silent.

ìBless my soul,î whispered the old bartender, ìHarry PotterÖ what an honor.î

He hurried out from behind the bar, rushed toward Harry and seized his hand, tears in his eyes.

ìWelcome back, Mr. Potter, welcome back.î

Harry didnít know what to say. Everyone was looking at him. The old woman with the pipe was puffing on it without realizing it had gone out. Hagrid was beaming.

Then there was a great scraping of chairs and the next moment, Harry found himself shaking hands with everyone in the Leaky Cauldron.

ìDoris Crockford, Mr. Potter, canít believe Iím meeting you at last.î

ìSo proud, Mr. Potter, Iím just so proud.î

ìAlways wanted to shake your handóIím all of a flutter.î

ìDelighted, Mr. Potter, just canít tell you, Diggleís the name, Dedalus Diggle.î

ìIíve seen you before!î said Harry, as Dedalus Diggleís top hat fell off in his excitement. ìYou bowed to me once in a shop.î

ìHe remembers!î cried Dedalus Diggle, looking around at everyone. ìDid you hear that? He remembers me!î Harry shook hands again and againóDoris Crockford kept coming back for more.

A pale young man made his way forward, very nervously. One of his eyes was twitching.

ìProfessor Quirrell!î said Hagrid. ìHarry, Professor Quirrell will be one of your teachers at Hogwarts.î

ìP-P-Potter,î stammered Professor Quirrell, grasping Harryís hand, ìc-canít t-tell you how p-pleased I am to meet you.î

ìWhat sort of magic do you teach, Professor Quirrell?î

ìD-Defense Against the D-D-Dark Arts,î muttered Professor Quirrell, as though heíd rather not think about it. ìN-not that you n-need it, eh, P-P-Potter?î He laughed nervously. ìYouíll be g-getting all your equipment, I suppose? Iíve g-got to p-pick up a new b-book on vampires, m-myself.î He looked terrified at the very thought.

But the others wouldnít let Professor Quirrell keep Harry to himself. It took almost ten minutes to get away from them all. At last, Hagrid managed to make himself heard over the babble.

ìMust get onólots ter buy. Come on, Harry.î

Doris Crockford shook Harryís hand one last time, and Hagrid led them through the bar and out into a small, walled courtyard, where there was nothing but a trash can and a few weeds.

Hagrid grinned at Harry.

ìTold yeh, didnít I? Told yeh you was famous. Even Professor Quirrell was trembliní ter meet yehómind you, heís usually trembliní.î

ìIs he always that nervous?î

ìOh, yeah. Poor bloke. Brilliant mind. He was fine while he was studyiní outta books but then he took a year off ter get some firsthand experienceÖ They say he met vampires in the Black Forest, and there was a nasty bit oí trouble with a hagónever been the same since. Scared of the students, scared of his own subject now, whereís me umbrella?î

Vampires? Hags? Harryís head was swimming. Hagrid, meanwhile, was counting bricks in the wall above the trash can.

ìThree upÖ two across,î he muttered. ìRight, stand back, Harry.î

He tapped the wall three times with the point of his umbrella.

The brick he had touched quiveredóit wriggledóin the middle, a small hole appearedóit grew wider and wideróa second later they were facing an archway large enough even for Hagrid, an archway onto a cobbled street that twisted and turned out of sight.

ìWelcome,î said Hagrid, ìto Diagon Alley.î

He grinned at Harryís amazement. They stepped through the archway. Harry looked quickly over his shoulder and saw the archway shrink instantly back into solid wall.

The sun shone brightly on a stack of cauldrons outside the nearest shop. CauldronsóAll SizesóCopper, Brass, Pewter, SilveróSelf StirringóCollapsible, said a sign hanging over them.

ìYeah, youíll be neediní one,î said Hagrid, ìbut we gotta get yer money first.î

Harry wished he had about eight more eyes. He turned his head in every direction as they walked up the street, trying to look at everything at once: the shops, the things outside them, the people doing their shopping. A plump woman outside an Apothecary was shaking her head as they passed, saying, ìDragon liver, seventeen Sickles an ounce, theyíre madÖî

A low, soft hooting came from a dark shop with a sign saying Eeylops Owl EmporiumóTawny, Screech, Barn, Brown, and Snowy. Several boys of about Harryís age had their noses pressed against a window with broomsticks in it. ìLook,î Harry heard one of them say, ìthe new Nimbus Two Thousandófastest everóî There were shops selling robes, shops selling telescopes and strange silver instruments Harry had never seen before, windows stacked with barrels of bat spleens and eelsí eyes, tottering piles of spell books, quills, and rolls of parchment, potion bottles, globes of the moonÖ

ìGringotts,î said Hagrid.

They had reached a snowy white building that towered over the other little shops. Standing beside its burnished bronze doors, wearing a uniform of scarlet and gold, wasó

ìYeah, thatís a goblin,î said Hagrid quietly as they walked up the white stone steps toward him. The goblin was about a head shorter than Harry. He had a swarthy, clever face, a pointed beard and, Harry noticed, very long fingers and feet. He bowed as they walked inside. Now they were facing a second pair of doors, silver this time, with words engraved upon them:

		Enter, stranger, but take heed
		Of what awaits the sin of greed,
		For those who take, but do not earn,
		Must pay most dearly in their turn.

		So if you seek beneath our floors
		A treasure that was never yours,
		Thief, you have been warned, beware
		Of finding more than treasure there.

ìLike I said, Yehíd be mad ter try aní rob it,î said Hagrid.

A pair of goblins bowed them through the silver doors and they were in a vast marble hall. About a hundred more goblins were sitting on high stools behind a long counter, scribbling in large ledgers, weighing coins in brass scales, examining precious stones through eyeglasses. There were too many doors to count leading off the hall, and yet more goblins were showing people in and out of these. Hagrid and Harry made for the counter.

ìMorning,î said Hagrid to a free goblin. ìWeíve come ter take some money outta Mr. Harry Potterís safe.î

ìYou have his key, sir?î

ìGot it here somewhere,î said Hagrid, and he started emptying his pockets onto the counter, scattering a handful of moldy dog biscuits over the goblinís book of numbers. The goblin wrinkled his nose. Harry watched the goblin on their right weighing a pile of rubies as big as glowing coals.

ìGot it,î said Hagrid at last, holding up a tiny golden key.

The goblin looked at it closely.

ìThat seems to be in order.î

ìAní Iíve also got a letter here from Professor Dumbledore,î said Hagrid importantly, throwing out his chest. ìItís about the You-Know-What in vault seven hundred and thirteen.î

The goblin read the letter carefully.

ìVery well,î he said, handing it back to Hagrid, ìI will have someone take you down to both vaults. Griphook!î

Griphook was yet another goblin. Once Hagrid had crammed all the dog biscuits back inside his pockets, he and Harry followed Griphook toward one of the doors leading off the hall.

ìWhatís the You-Know-What in vault seven hundred and thirteen?î Harry asked.

ìCanít tell yeh that,î said Hagrid mysteriously. ìVery secret. Hogwarts business. Dumbledoreís trusted me. More ín my jobís worth ter tell yeh that.î

Griphook held the door open for them. Harry, who had expected more marble, was surprised. They were in a narrow stone passageway lit with flaming torches. It sloped steeply downward and there were little railway tracks on the floor. Griphook whistled and a small cart came hurtling up the tracks toward them. They climbed inóHagrid with some difficultyóand were off.

At first they just hurtled through a maze of twisting passages. Harry tried to remember, left, right, right, left, middle fork, right, left, but it was impossible. The rattling cart seemed to know its own way, because Griphook wasnít steering.

Harryís eyes stung as the cold air rushed past them, but he kept them wide open. Once, he thought he saw a burst of fire at the end of a passage and twisted around to see if it was a dragon, but too lateóthey plunged even deeper, passing an underground lake where huge stalactites and stalagmites grew from the ceiling and floor.

ìI never know,î Harry called to Hagrid over the noise of the cart, ìwhatís the difference between a stalagmite and a stalactite?î

ìStalagmiteís got an ëmí in it,î said Hagrid. ìAní doní ask me questions just now, I think Iím gonna be sick.î

He did look very green, and when the cart stopped at last beside a small door in the passage wall, Hagrid got out and had to lean against the wall to stop his knees from trembling.

Griphook unlocked the door. A lot of green smoke came billowing out, and as it cleared, Harry gasped. Inside were mounds of gold coins. Columns of silver. Heaps of little bronze Knuts.

ìAll yours,î smiled Hagrid.

All Harryísóit was incredible. The Dursleys couldnít have known about this or theyíd have had it from him faster than blinking. How often had they complained how much Harry cost them to keep? And all the time there had been a small fortune belonging to him, buried deep under London.

Hagrid helped Harry pile some of it into a bag.

ìThe gold ones are Galleons,î he explained. ìSeventeen silver Sickles to a Galleon and twenty nine Knuts to a Sickle, itís easy enough. Right, that should be enough fer a couple oí terms, weíll keep the rest safe for yeh.î He turned to Griphook. ìVault seven hundred and thirteen now, please, and can we go more slowly?î

ìOne speed only,î said Griphook.

They were going even deeper now and gathering speed. The air became colder and colder as they hurtled round tight corners. They went rattling over an underground ravine, and Harry leaned over the side to try to see what was down at the dark bottom, but Hagrid groaned and pulled him back by the scruff of his neck.

Vault seven hundred and thirteen had no keyhole.

ìStand back,î said Griphook importantly. He stroked the door gently with one of his long fingers and it simply melted away.

ìIf anyone but a Gringotts goblin tried that, theyíd be sucked through the door and trapped in there,î said Griphook.

ìHow often do you check to see if anyoneís inside?î Harry asked.

ìAbout once every ten years,î said Griphook with a rather nasty grin.

Something really extraordinary had to be inside this top security vault, Harry was sure, and he leaned forward eagerly, expecting to see fabulous jewels at the very leastóbut at first he thought it was empty. Then he noticed a grubby little package wrapped up in brown paper lying on the floor. Hagrid picked it up and tucked it deep inside his coat. Harry longed to know what it was, but knew better than to ask.

ìCome on, back in this infernal cart, and donít talk to me on the way back, itís best if I keep me mouth shut,î said Hagrid.

One wild cart ride later they stood blinking in the sunlight outside Gringotts. Harry didnít know where to run first now that he had a bag full of money. He didnít have to know how many Galleons there were to a pound to know that he was holding more money than heíd had in his whole lifeómore money than even Dudley had ever had.

ìMight as well get yer uniform,î said Hagrid, nodding toward Madam Malkinís Robes for All Occasions. ìListen, Harry, would yeh mind if I slipped off fer a pick me up in the Leaky Cauldron? I hate them Gringotts carts.î He did still look a bit sick, so Harry entered Madam Malkinís shop alone, feeling nervous.

Madam Malkin was a squat, smiling witch dressed all in mauve.

ìHogwarts, dear?î she said, when Harry started to speak. ìGot the lot hereóanother young man being fitted up just now, in fact.î

In the back of the shop, a boy with a pale, pointed face was standing on a footstool while a second witch pinned up his long black robes. Madam Malkin stood Harry on a stool next to him) slipped a long robe over his head, and began to pin it to the right length.

ìHello,î said the boy, ìHogwarts, too?î

ìYes,î said Harry.

ìMy fatherís next door buying my books and motherís up the street looking at wands,î said the boy. He had a bored, drawling voice. ìThen Iím going to drag them off to took at racing brooms. I donít see why first years canít have their own. I think Iíll bully father into getting me one and Iíll smuggle it in somehow.î

Harry was strongly reminded of Dudley.

ìHave you got your own broom?î the boy went on.

ìNo,î said Harry.

ìPlay Quidditch at all?î

ìNo,î Harry said again, wondering what on earth Quidditch could be.

ìI doóFather says itís a crime if Iím not picked to play for my house, and I must say, I agree. Know what house youíll be in yet?î

ìNo,î said Harry, feeling more stupid by the minute.

ìWell, no one really knows until they get there, do they, but I know Iíll be in Slytherin, all our family have beenóimagine being in Hufflepuff, I think Iíd leave, wouldnít you?î

ìMmm,î said Harry, wishing he could say something a bit more interesting.

ìI say, look at that man!î said the boy suddenly, nodding toward the front window. Hagrid was standing there, grinning at Harry and pointing at two large ice creams to show he couldnít come in.

ìThatís Hagrid,î said Harry, pleased to know something the boy didnít. ìHe works at Hogwarts.î

ìOh,î said the boy, ìIíve heard of him. Heís a sort of servant, isnít he?î

ìHeís the gamekeeper,î said Harry. He was liking the boy less and less every second.

ìYes, exactly. I heard heís a sort of savageólives in a hut on the school grounds and every now and then he gets drunk, tries to do magic, and ends up setting fire to his bed.î

ìI think heís brilliant,î said Harry coldly.

ìDo you?î said the boy, with a slight sneer. ìWhy is he with you? Where are your parents?î

ìTheyíre dead,î said Harry shortly. He didnít feel much like going into the matter with this boy.

ìOh, sorry,î said the other, not sounding sorry at all. ìBut they were our kind, werenít they?î

ìThey were a witch and wizard, if thatís what you mean.î

ìI really donít think they should let the other sort in, do you? Theyíre just not the same, theyíve never been brought up to know our ways. Some of them have never even heard of Hogwarts until they get the letter, imagine. I think they should keep it in the old wizarding families. Whatís your surname, anyway?î

But before Harry could answer, Madam Malkin said, ìThatís you done, my dear,î and Harry, not sorry for an excuse to stop talking to the boy, hopped down from the footstool.

ìWell, Iíll see you at Hogwarts, I suppose,î said the drawling boy.

Harry was rather quiet as he ate the ice cream Hagrid had bought him (chocolate and raspberry with chopped nuts).

ìWhatís up?î said Hagrid.

ìNothing,î Harry lied. They stopped to buy parchment and quills. Harry cheered up a bit when he found a bottle of ink that changed color as you wrote. When they had left the shop, he said, ìHagrid, whatís Quidditch?î

ìBlimey, Harry, I keep forgettiní how little yeh knowónot knowiní about Quidditch!î

ìDonít make me feel worse,î said Harry. He told Hagrid about the pate boy in Madam Malkinís.

ìóand he said people from Muggle families shouldnít even be allowed in.î

ìYer not from a Muggle family. If heíd known who yeh wereóheís grown up knowiní yer name if his parents are wizardiní folk. You saw what everyone in the Leaky Cauldron was like when they saw yeh. Anyway, what does he know about it, some oí the best I ever saw were the only ones with magic in íem in a long line oí Mugglesólook at yer mum! Look what she had fer a sister!î

ìSo what is Quidditch?î

ìItís our sport. Wizard sport. Itís likeólike soccer in the Muggle worldóeveryone follows Quidditchóplayed up in the air on broomsticks and thereís four ballsósorta hard ter explain the rules.î

ìAnd what are Slytherin and Hufflepuff?î

ìSchool houses. Thereís four. Everyone says Hufflepuff are a lot oí duffers, butóî

ìI bet Iím in Hufflepuff,î said Harry gloomily.

ìBetter Hufflepuff than Slytherin,î said Hagrid darkly. ìThereís not a single witch or wizard who went bad who wasnít in Slytherin. You-Know-Who was one.î

ìVolósorryóYou-Know-Who was at Hogwarts?î

ìYears aní years ago,î said Hagrid.

They bought Harryís school books in a shop called Flourish and Blotts where the shelves were stacked to the ceiling with books as large as paving stones bound in leather; books the size of postage stamps in covers of silk; books full of peculiar symbols and a few books with nothing in them at all. Even Dudley, who never read anything, would have been wild to get his hands on some of these. Hagrid almost had to drag Harry away from Curses and Countercurses (Bewitch Your Friends and Befuddle Your Enemies with the Latest Revenges: Hair Loss, Jelly Legs, Tongue-Tying and Much, Much More) by Professor Vindictus Viridian.

ìI was trying to find out how to curse Dudley.î

ìIím not sayiní thatís not a good idea, but yer not ter use magic in the Muggle world except in very special circumstances,î said Hagrid. ìAní anyway, yeh couldní work any of the curses yet, yehíll need a lot more study before yeh get ter that level.î

Hagrid wouldnít let Harry buy a solid gold cauldron, either (ìIt says pewter on yer listî), but they got a nice set of scales for weighing potion ingredients and a collapsible brass telescope. Then they visited the Apothecary, which was fascinating enough to make up for its horrible smell, a mixture of bad eggs and rotted cabbages. Barrels of slimy stuff stood on the floor; jars of herbs, dried roots, and bright powders lined the walls; bundles of feathers, strings of fangs, and snarled claws hung from the ceiling. While Hagrid asked the man behind the counter for a supply of some basic potion ingredients for Harry, Harry himself examined silver unicorn horns at twenty one Galleons each and minuscule, glittery black beetle eyes (five Knuts a scoop).

Outside the Apothecary, Hagrid checked Harryís list again.

ìJust yer wand leftóA yeah, aní I still havenít got yeh a birthday present.î

Harry felt himself go red.

ìYou donít have toóî

ìI know I donít have to. Tell yeh what, Iíll get yer animal. Not a toad, toads went outta fashion years ago, yehíd be laughed atóaní I doní like cats, they make me sneeze. Iíll get yer an owl. All the kids want owls, theyíre dead useful, carry yer mail aní everythiní.î

Twenty minutes later, they left Eeylops Owl Emporium, which had been dark and full of rustling and flickering, jewel bright eyes. Harry now carried a large cage that held a beautiful snowy owl, fast asleep with her head under her wing. He couldnít stop stammering his thanks, sounding just like Professor Quirrell.

ìDoní mention it,î said Hagrid gruffly. ìDoní expect youíve had a lotta presents from them Dursleys. Just Ollivanderís left nowóonly place fer wands, Ollivanderís, and yeh gotta have the best wand.î

A magic wandÖ this was what Harry had been really looking forward to.

The last shop was narrow and shabby. Peeling gold letters over the door read Ollivanders: Makers of Fine Wands since 382 B.C. A single wand lay on a faded purple cushion in the dusty window.

A tinkling bell rang somewhere in the depths of the shop as they stepped inside. It was a tiny place, empty except for a single, spindly chair that Hagrid sat on to wait. Harry felt strangely as though he had entered a very strict library; he swallowed a lot of new questions that had just occurred to him and looked instead at the thousands of narrow boxes piled neatly right up to the ceiling. For some reason, the back of his neck prickled. The very dust and silence in here seemed to tingle with some secret magic.

ìGood afternoon,î said a soft voice. Harry jumped. Hagrid must have jumped, too, because there was a loud crunching noise and he got quickly off the spindly chair.

An old man was standing before them, his wide, pale eyes shining like moons through the gloom of the shop.

ìHello,î said Harry awkwardly.

ìAh yes,î said the man. ìYes, yes. I thought Iíd be seeing you soon. Harry Potter.î It wasnít a question. ìYou have your motherís eyes. It seems only yesterday she was in here herself, buying her first wand. Ten and a quarter inches long, swishy, made of willow. Nice wand for charm work.î

Mr. Ollivander moved closer to Harry. Harry wished he would blink. Those silvery eyes were a bit creepy.

ìYour father, on the other hand, favored a mahogany wand. Eleven inches. Pliable. A little more power and excellent for transfiguration. Well, I say your father favored itóitís really the wand that chooses the wizard, of course.î

Mr. Ollivander had come so close that he and Harry were almost nose to nose. Harry could see himself reflected in those misty eyes.

ìAnd thatís whereÖî

Mr. Ollivander touched the lightning scar on Harryís forehead with a long, white finger.

ìIím sorry to say I sold the wand that did it,î he said softly. ìThirteen and a half inches. Yew. Powerful wand, very powerful, and in the wrong handsÖ well, if Iíd known what that wand was going out into the world to doÖî

He shook his head and then, to Harryís relief, spotted Hagrid.

ìRubeus! Rubeus Hagrid! How nice to see you againÖ Oak, sixteen inches, rather bendy, wasnít it?î

ìIt was, sir, yes,î said Hagrid.

ìGood wand, that one. But I suppose they snapped it in half when you got expelled?î said Mr. Ollivander, suddenly stern.

ìEróyes, they did, yes,î said Hagrid, shuffling his feet. ìIíve still got the pieces, though,î he added brightly.

ìBut you donít use them?î said Mr. Ollivander sharply.

ìOh, no, sir,î said Hagrid quickly. Harry noticed he gripped his pink umbrella very tightly as he spoke.

ìHmmm,î said Mr. Ollivander, giving Hagrid a piercing look. ìWell, nowóMr. Potter. Let me see.î He pulled a long tape measure with silver markings out of his pocket. ìWhich is your wand arm?î

ìErówell, Iím right handed,î said Harry.

ìHold out your arm. Thatís it.î He measured Harry from shoulder to finger, then wrist to elbow, shoulder to floor, knee to armpit and round his head. As he measured, he said, ìEvery Ollivander wand has a core of a powerful magical substance, Mr. Potter. We use unicorn hairs, phoenix tail feathers, and the heartstrings of dragons. No two Ollivander wands are the same, just as no two unicorns, dragons, or phoenixes are quite the same. And of course, you will never get such good results with another wizardís wand.î

Harry suddenly realized that the tape measure, which was measuring between his nostrils, was doing this on its own. Mr. Ollivander was flitting around the shelves, taking down boxes.

ìThat will do,î he said, and the tape measure crumpled into a heap on the floor. ìRight then, Mr. Potter. Try this one. Beechwood and dragon heartstring. Nine inches. Nice and flexible. just take it and give it a wave.î

Harry took the wand and (feeling foolish) waved it around a bit, but Mr. Ollivander snatched it out of his hand almost at once.

ìMaple and phoenix feather. Seven inches. Quite whippy. Tryóî

Harry triedóbut he had hardly raised the wand when it, too, was snatched back by Mr. Ollivander.

ìNo, no here, ebony and unicorn hair, eight and a half inches, springy. Go on, go on, try it out.î

Harry tried. And tried. He had no idea what Mr. Ollivander was waiting for. The pile of tried wands was mounting higher and higher on the spindly chair, but the more wands Mr. Ollivander pulled from the shelves, the happier he seemed to become.

ìTricky customer, eh? Not to worry, weíll find the perfect match here somewhereóI wonder, nowóyes, why notóunusual combinationóholly and phoenix feather, eleven inches, nice and supple.î

Harry took the wand. He felt a sudden warmth in his fingers. He raised the wand above his head, brought it swishing down through the dusty air and a stream of red and gold sparks shot from the end like a firework, throwing dancing spots of light on to the walls. Hagrid whooped and clapped and Mr. Ollivander cried, ìOh, bravo! Yes, indeed, oh, very good. Well, well, wellÖ how curiousÖ how very curiousÖî

He put Harryís wand back into its box and wrapped it in brown paper, still muttering, ìCuriousÖ curiousÖî

ìSorry,î said Harry, ìbutwhatís curious?î

Mr. Ollivander fixed Harry with his pale stare.

ìI remember every wand Iíve ever sold, Mr. Potter. Every single wand. It so happens that the phoenix whose tail feather is in your wand, gave another featherójust one other. It is very curious indeed that you should be destined for this wand when itís brotherówhy, itís brother gave you that scar.î

Harry swallowed.

ìYes, thirteen and a half inches. Yew. Curious indeed how these things happen. The wand chooses the wizard, rememberÖ I think we must expect great things from you, Mr. PotterÖ After all, He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named did great thingsóterrible, yes, but great.î

Harry shivered. He wasnít sure he liked Mr. Ollivander too much. He paid seven gold Galleons for his wand, and Mr. Ollivander bowed them from his shop.

The late afternoon sun hung low in the sky as Harry and Hagrid made their way back down Diagon Alley, back through the wall, back through the Leaky Cauldron, now empty. Harry didnít speak at all as they walked down the road; he didnít even notice how much people were gawking at them on the Underground, laden as they were with all their funny shaped packages, with the snowy owl asleep in its cage on Harryís lap. Up another escalator, out into Paddington station; Harry only realized where they were when Hagrid tapped him on the shoulder.

ìGot time fer a bite to eat before yer train leaves,î he said.

He bought Harry a hamburger and they sat down on plastic seats to eat them. Harry kept looking around. Everything looked so strange, somehow.

ìYou all right, Harry? Yer very quiet,î said Hagrid.

Harry wasnít sure he could explain. Heíd just had the best birthday of his lifeóand yetóhe chewed his hamburger, trying to find the words.

ìEveryone thinks Iím special,î he said at last. ìAll those people in the Leaky Cauldron, Professor Quirrell, Mr. OllivanderÖ but I donít know anything about magic at all. How can they expect great things? Iím famous and I canít even remember what Iím famous for. I donít know what happened when VolósorryóI mean, the night my parents died.î

Hagrid leaned across the table. Behind the wild beard and eyebrows he wore a very kind smile.

ìDoní you worry, Harry. Youíll learn fast enough. Everyone starts at the beginning at Hogwarts, youíll be just fine. Just be yerself. I know itís hard. Yehíve been singled out, aní thatís always hard. But yehíll have a great time at HogwartsóI didóstill do, ísmatter of fact.î

Hagrid helped Harry on to the train that would take him back to the Dursleys, then handed him an envelope.

ìYer ticket fer Hogwarts,î he said. ìFirst oí SeptemberóKingís Crossóitís all on yer ticket. Any problems with the Dursleys, send me a letter with yer owl, sheíll know where to find meÖ See yeh soon, Harry.î

The train pulled out of the station. Harry wanted to watch Hagrid until he was out of sight; he rose in his seat and pressed his nose against the window, but he blinked and Hagrid had gone.




6. THE JOURNEY FROM PLATFORM NINE AND THREE QUARTERS


Harryís last month with the Dursleys wasnít fun. True, Dudley was now so scared of Harry he wouldnít stay in the same room, while Aunt Petunia and Uncle Vernon didnít shut Harry in his cupboard, force him to do anything, or shout at himóin fact, they didnít speak to him at all. Half terrified, half furious, they acted as though any chair with Harry in it were empty. Although this was an improvement in many ways, it did become a bit depressing after a while.

Harry kept to his room, with his new owl for company. He had decided to call her Hedwig, a name he had found in A History of Magic. His school books were very interesting. He lay on his bed reading late into the night, Hedwig swooping in and out of the open window as she pleased. It was lucky that Aunt Petunia didnít come in to vacuum anymore, because Hedwig kept bringing back dead mice. Every night before he went to sleep, Harry ticked off another day on the piece of paper he had pinned to the wall, counting down to September the first.

On the last day of August he thought heíd better speak to his aunt and uncle about getting to Kingís Cross station the next day, so he went down to the living room where they were watching a quiz show on television. He cleared his throat to let them know he was there, and Dudley screamed and ran from the room.

ìEróUncle Vernon?î

Uncle Vernon grunted to show he was listening.

ìEróI need to be at Kingís Cross tomorrow toóto go to Hogwarts.î

Uncle Vernon grunted again.

ìWould it be all right if you gave me a lift?î

Grunt. Harry supposed that meant yes.

ìThank you.î

He was about to go back upstairs when Uncle Vernon actually spoke.

ìFunny way to get to a wizardsí school, the train. Magic carpets all got punctures, have they?î

Harry didnít say anything.

ìWhere is this school, anyway?î

ìI donít know,î said Harry, realizing this for the first time. He pulled the ticket Hagrid had given him out of his pocket.

ìI just take the train from platform nine and three-quarters at eleven oíclock,î he read.

His aunt and uncle stared.

ìPlatform what?î

ìNine and three-quarters.î

ìDonít talk rubbish,î said Uncle Vernon. ìThere is no platform nine and three-quarters.î

ìItís on my ticket.î

ìBarking,î said Uncle Vernon, ìhowling mad, the lot of them. Youíll see. You just wait. All right, weíll take you to Kingís Cross. Weíre going up to London tomorrow anyway, or I wouldnít bother.î

ìWhy are you going to London?î Harry asked, trying to keep things friendly.

ìTaking Dudley to the hospital,î growled Uncle Vernon. ìGot to have that ruddy tail removed before he goes to Smeltings.î

Harry woke at five oíclock the next morning and was too excited and nervous to go back to sleep. He got up and pulled on his jeans because he didnít want to walk into the station in his wizardís robesóheíd change on the train. He checked his Hogwarts list yet again to make sure he had everything he needed, saw that Hedwig was shut safely in her cage, and then paced the room, waiting for the Dursleys to get up. Two hours later, Harryís huge, heavy trunk had been loaded into the Dursleysí car, Aunt Petunia had talked Dudley into sitting next to Harry, and they had set off.

They reached Kingís Cross at half past ten. Uncle Vernon dumped Harryís trunk onto a cart and wheeled it into the station for him. Harry thought this was strangely kind until Uncle Vernon stopped dead, facing the platforms with a nasty grin on his face.

ìWell, there you are, boy. Platform nineóplatform ten. Your platform should be somewhere in the middle, but they donít seem to have built it yet, do they?î

He was quite right, of course. There was a big plastic number nine over one platform and a big plastic number ten over the one next to it, and in the middle, nothing at all.

ìHave a good term,î said Uncle Vernon with an even nastier smile. He left without another word. Harry turned and saw the Dursleys drive away. All three of them were laughing. Harryís mouth went rather dry. What on earth was he going to do? He was starting to attract a lot of funny looks, because of Hedwig. Heíd have to ask someone.

He stopped a passing guard, but didnít dare mention platform nine and three-quarters. The guard had never heard of Hogwarts and when Harry couldnít even tell him what part of the country it was in, he started to get annoyed, as though Harry was being stupid on purpose. Getting desperate, Harry asked for the train that left at eleven oíclock, but the guard said there wasnít one. In the end the guard strode away, muttering about time wasters. Harry was now trying hard not to panic. According to the large clock over the arrivals board, he had ten minutes left to get on the train to Hogwarts and he had no idea how to do it; he was stranded in the middle of a station with a trunk he could hardly lift, a pocket full of wizard money, and a large owl.

Hagrid must have forgotten to tell him something you had to do, like tapping the third brick on the left to get into Diagon Alley. He wondered if he should get out his wand and start tapping the ticket inspectorís stand between platforms nine and ten.

At that moment a group of people passed just behind him and he caught a few words of what they were saying.

ìópacked with Muggles, of courseóî

Harry swung round. The speaker was a plump woman who was talking to four boys, all with flaming red hair. Each of them was pushing a trunk like Harryís in front of himóand they had an owl.

Heart hammering, Harry pushed his cart after them. They stopped and so did he, just near enough to hear what they were saying.

ìNow, whatís the platform number?î said the boysí mother.

ìNine and three-quarters!î piped a small girl, also red headed, who was holding her hand, ìMom, canít I goÖî

ìYouíre not old enough, Ginny, now be quiet. All right, Percy, you go first.î

What looked like the oldest boy marched toward platforms nine and ten. Harry watched, careful not to blink in case he missed itóbut just as the boy reached the dividing barrier between the two platforms, a large crowd of tourists came swarming in front of him and by the time the last backpack had cleared away, the boy had vanished.

ìFred, you next,î the plump woman said.

ìIím not Fred, Iím George,î said the boy. ìHonestly, woman, you call yourself our mother? Canít you tell Iím George?î

ìSorry, George, dear.î

ìOnly joking, I am Fred,î said the boy, and off he went. His twin called after him to hurry up, and he must have done so, because a second later, he had goneóbut how had he done it?

Now the third brother was walking briskly toward the barrier he was almost thereóand then, quite suddenly, he wasnít anywhere.

There was nothing else for it.

ìExcuse me,î Harry said to the plump woman.

ìHello, dear,î she said. ìFirst time at Hogwarts? Ronís new, too.î

She pointed at the last and youngest of her sons. He was tall, thin, and gangling, with freckles, big hands and feet, and a long nose.

ìYes,î said Harry. ìThe thing isóthe thing is, I donít know how toóî

ìHow to get onto the platform?î she said kindly, and Harry nodded.

ìNot to worry,î she said. ìAll you have to do is walk straight at the barrier between platforms nine and ten. Donít stop and donít be scared youíll crash into it, thatís very important. Best do it at a bit of a run if youíre nervous. Go on, go now before Ron.î

ìEróokay,î said Harry.

He pushed his trolley around and stared at the barrier. It looked very solid.

He started to walk toward it. People jostled him on their way to platforms nine and ten. Harry walked more quickly. He was going to smash right into that barrier and then heíd be in troubleóleaning forward on his cart, he broke into a heavy runóthe barrier was coming nearer and neareróhe wouldnít be able to stopóthe cart was out of controlóhe was a foot awayóhe closed his eyes ready for the crashó

It didnít comeÖ he kept on runningÖ he opened his eyes. A scarlet steam engine was waiting next to a platform packed with people. A sign overhead said Hogwarts Express, eleven oíclock. Harry looked behind him and saw a wrought iron archway where the barrier had been, with the words Platform Nine and Three-quarters on it, He had done it.

Smoke from the engine drifted over the heads of the chattering crowd, while cats of every color wound here and there between their legs. Owls hooted to one another in a disgruntled sort of way over the babble and the scraping of heavy trunks.

The first few carriages were already packed with students, some hanging out of the window to talk to their families, some fighting over seats. Harry pushed his cart off down the platform in search of an empty seat. He passed a round faced boy who was saying, ìGran, Iíve lost my toad again.î

ìOh, Neville,î he heard the old woman sigh.

A boy with dreadlocks was surrounded by a small crowd.

ìGive us a look, Lee, go on.î

The boy lifted the lid of a box in his arms, and the people around him shrieked and yelled as something inside poked out a long, hairy leg.

Harry pressed on through the crowd until he found an empty compartment near the end of the train. He put Hedwig inside first and then started to shove and heave his trunk toward the train door. He tried to lift it up the steps but could hardly raise one end and twice he dropped it painfully on his foot.

ìWant a hand?î It was one of the red haired twins heíd followed through the barrier.

ìYes, please,î Harry panted.

ìOy, Fred! Címere and help!î

With the twinsí help, Harryís trunk was at last tucked away in a corner of the compartment.

ìThanks,î said Harry, pushing his sweaty hair out of his eyes.

ìWhatís that?î said one of the twins suddenly, pointing at Harryís lightning scar.

ìBlimey,î said the other twin. ìAre youóî

ìHe is,î said the first twin. ìArenít you?î he added to Harry.

ìWhat?î said Harry.

ìHarry Potter,î chorused the twins.

ìOh, him,î said Harry. ìI mean, yes, I am.î

The two boys gawked at him, and Harry felt himself turning red. Then, to his relief, a voice came floating in through the trainís open door.

ìFred? George? Are you there?î

ìComing, Mom.î

With a last look at Harry, the twins hopped off the train.

Harry sat down next to the window where, half hidden, he could watch the red haired family on the platform and hear what they were saying. Their mother had just taken out her handkerchief.

ìRon, youíve got something on your nose.î

The youngest boy tried to jerk out of the way, but she grabbed him and began rubbing the end of his nose.

ìMomógeroffóî He wriggled free.

ìAaah, has ickle Ronnie got somefink on his nosie?î said one of the twins.

ìShut up,î said Ron.

ìWhereís Percy?î said their mother.

ìHeís coming now.î

The oldest boy came striding into sight. He had already changed into his billowing black Hogwarts robes, and Harry noticed a shiny silver badge on his chest with the letter P on it.

ìCanít stay long, Mother,î he said. ìIím up front, the prefects have got two compartments to themselvesóî

ìOh, are you a prefect, Percy?î said one of the twins, with an air of great surprise. ìYou should have said something, we had no idea.î

ìHang on, I think I remember him saying something about it,î said the other twin. ìOnceóî

ìOr twiceóî

ìA minuteóî

ìAll summeróî

ìOh, shut up,î said Percy the Prefect.

ìHow come Percy gets new robes, anyway?î said one of the twins.

ìBecause heís a prefect,î said their mother fondly. ìAll right, dear, well, have a good termósend me an owl when you get there.î

She kissed Percy on the cheek and he left. Then she turned to the twins.

ìNow, you twoóthis year, you behave yourselves. If I get one more owl telling me youíveóyouíve blown up a toilet oróî

ìBlown up a toilet? Weíve never blown up a toilet.î

ìGreat idea though, thanks, Mom.î

ìItís not funny. And look after Ron.î

ìDonít worry, ickle Ronniekins is safe with us.î

ìShut up,î said Ron again. He was almost as tall as the twins already and his nose was still pink where his mother had rubbed it.

ìHey, Mom, guess what? Guess who we just met on the train?î

Harry leaned back quickly so they couldnít see him looking.

ìYou know that black haired boy who was near us in the station? Know who he is?î

ìWho?î

ìHarry Potter!î

Harry heard the little girlís voice.

ìOh, Mom, can I go on the train and see him, Mom, eh pleaseÖî

ìYouíve already seen him, Ginny, and the poor boy isnít something you goggle at in a zoo. Is he really, Fred? How do you know?î

ìAsked him. Saw his scar. Itís really thereólike lightning.î

ìPoor dearóno wonder he was alone, I wondered. He was ever so polite when he asked how to get onto the platform.î

ìNever mind that, do you think he remembers what You-Know-Who looks like?î

Their mother suddenly became very stern.

ìI forbid you to ask him, Fred. No, donít you dare. As though he needs reminding of that on his first day at school.î

ìAll right, keep your hair on.î

A whistle sounded.

ìHurry up!î their mother said, and the three boys clambered onto the train. They leaned out of the window for her to kiss them good bye, and their younger sister began to cry.

ìDonít, Ginny, weíll send you loads of owls.î

ìWeíll send you a Hogwarts toilet seat.î

ìGeorge!î

ìOnly joking, Mom.î

The train began to move. Harry saw the boysí mother waving and their sister, half laughing, half crying, running to keep up with the train until it gathered too much speed, then she fell back and waved.

Harry watched the girl and her mother disappear as the train rounded the corner. Houses flashed past the window. Harry felt a great leap of excitement. He didnít know what he was going to but it had to be better than what he was leaving behind.

The door of the compartment slid open and the youngest redheaded boy came in.

ìAnyone sitting there?î he asked, pointing at the seat opposite Harry. ìEverywhere else is full.î

Harry shook his head and the boy sat down. He glanced at Harry and then looked quickly out of the window, pretending he hadnít looked. Harry saw he still had a black mark on his nose.

ìHey, Ron.î

The twins were back.

ìListen, weíre going down the middle of the trainóLee Jordanís got a giant tarantula down there.î

ìRight,î mumbled Ron.

ìHarry,î said the other twin, ìdid we introduce ourselves? Fred and George Weasley. And this is Ron, our brother. See you later, then.î

ìBye,î said Harry and Ron. The twins slid the compartment door shut behind them.

ìAre you really Harry Potter?î Ron blurted out.

Harry nodded.

ìOh well, I thought it might be one of Fred and Georgeís jokes,î said Ron. ìAnd have you really gotóyou knowÖî

He pointed at Harryís forehead.

Harry pulled back his bangs to show the lightning scar. Ron stared.

ìSo thatís where You-Know-Whoó?î

ìYes,î said Harry, ìbut I canít remember it.î

ìNothing?î said Ron eagerly.

ìWellóI remember a lot of green light, but nothing else.î

ìWow,î said Ron. He sat and stared at Harry for a few moments, then, as though he had suddenly realized what he was doing, he looked quickly out of the window again.

ìAre all your family wizards?î asked Harry, who found Ron just as interesting as Ron found him.

ìEróYes, I think so,î said Ron. ìI think Momís got a second cousin whoís an accountant, but we never talk about him.î

ìSo you must know loads of magic already.î

The Weasleys were clearly one of those old wizarding families the pale boy in Diagon Alley had talked about.

ìI heard you went to live with Muggles,î said Ron. ìWhat are they like?î

ìHorribleówell, not all of them. My aunt and uncle and cousin are, though. Wish Iíd had three wizard brothers.î

ìFive,î said Ron. For some reason, he was looking gloomy. ìIím the sixth in our family to go to Hogwarts. You could say Iíve got a lot to live up to. Bill and Charlie have already leftóBill was head boy and Charlie was captain of Quidditch. Now Percyís a prefect. Fred and George mess around a lot, but they still get really good marks and everyone thinks theyíre really funny. Everyone expects me to do as well as the others, but if I do, itís no big deal, because they did it first. You never get anything new, either, with five brothers. Iíve got Billís old robes, Charlieís old wand, and Percyís old rat.î

Ron reached inside his jacket and pulled out a fat gray rat, which was asleep.

ìHis nameís Scabbers and heís useless, he hardly ever wakes up. Percy got an owl from my dad for being made a prefect, but they couldnít affóI mean, I got Scabbers instead.î

Ronís ears went pink. He seemed to think heíd said too much, because he went back to staring out of the window.

Harry didnít think there was anything wrong with not being able to afford an owl. After all, heíd never had any money in his life until a month ago, and he told Ron so, all about having to wear Dudleyís old clothes and never getting proper birthday presents. This seemed to cheer Ron up.

ìÖand until Hagrid told me, I didnít know anything about being a wizard or about my parents or Voldemortóî

Ron gasped.

ìWhat?î said Harry.

ìYou said You-Know-Whoís name!î said Ron, sounding both shocked and impressed. ìIíd have thought you, of all peopleóî

ìIím not trying to be brave or anything, saying the name,î said Harry, ìI just never knew you shouldnít. See what I mean? Iíve got loads to learnÖ I bet,î he added, voicing for the first time something that had been worrying him a lot lately, ìI bet Iím the worst in the class.î

ìYou wonít be. Thereís loads of people who come from Muggle families and they learn quick enough.î

While they had been talking, the train had carried them out of London. Now they were speeding past fields full of cows and sheep. They were quiet for a time, watching the fields and lanes flick past.

Around half past twelve there was a great clattering outside in the corridor and a smiling, dimpled woman slid back their door and said, ìAnything off the cart, dears?î

Harry, who hadnít had any breakfast, leapt to his feet, but Ronís ears went pink again and he muttered that heíd brought sandwiches. Harry went out into the corridor.

He had never had any money for candy with the Dursleys, and now that he had pockets rattling with gold and silver he was ready to buy as many Mars Bars as he could carryóbut the woman didnít have Mars Bars. What she did have were Bettie Bottís Every Flavor Beans, Droobleís Best Blowing Gum, Chocolate Frogs. Pumpkin Pasties, Cauldron Cakes, Licorice Wands, and a number of other strange things Harry had never seen in his life. Not wanting to miss anything, he got some of everything and paid the woman eleven silver Sickles and seven bronze Knuts.

Ron stared as Harry brought it all back in to the compartment and tipped it onto an empty seat.

ìHungry, are you?î

ìStarving,î said Harry, taking a large bite out of a pumpkin pasty.

Ron had taken out a lumpy package and unwrapped it. There were four sandwiches inside. He pulled one of them apart and said, ìShe always forgets I donít like corned beef.î

ìSwap you for one of these,î said Harry, holding up a pasty. ìGo onóî

ìYou donít want this, itís all dry,î said Ron. ìShe hasnít got much time,î he added quickly, ìyou know, with five of us.î

ìGo on, have a pasty,î said Harry, who had never had anything to share before or, indeed, anyone to share it with. It was a nice feeling, sitting there with Ron, eating their way through all Harryís pasties, cakes, and candies (the sandwiches lay forgotten).

ìWhat are these?î Harry asked Ron, holding up a pack of Chocolate Frogs. ìTheyíre not really frogs, are they?î He was starting to feel that nothing would surprise him.

ìNo,î said Ron. ìBut see what the card is. Iím missing Agrippa.î

ìWhat?î

ìOh, of course, you wouldnít knowóChocolate Frogs have cards, inside them, you know, to collectófamous witches and wizards. Iíve got about five hundred, but I havenít got Agrippa or Ptolemy.î

Harry unwrapped his Chocolate Frog and picked up the card. It showed a manís face. He wore half-moon glasses, had a long, crooked nose, and flowing silver hair, beard, and mustache. Underneath the picture was the name Albus Dumbledore.

ìSo this is Dumbledore!î said Harry.

ìDonít tell me youíd never heard of Dumbledore!î said Ron. ìCan I have a frog? I might get Agrippaóthanksóî

Harry turned over his card and read:



ALBUS DUMBLEDORE

CURRENTLY HEADMASTER OF HOGWARTS

Considered by many the greatest wizard of modern times, Dumbledore is particularly famous for his defeat of the dark wizard Grindelwald in 1945, for the discovery of the twelve uses of dragonís blood, and his work on alchemy with his partner, Nicolas Flamel. Professor Dumbledore enjoys chamber music and tenpin bowling.


Harry turned the card back over and saw, to his astonishment, that Dumbledoreís face had disappeared.

ìHeís gone!î

ìWell, you canít expect him to hang around all day,î said Ron. ìHeíll be back. No, Iíve got Morgana again and Iíve got about six of herÖ do you want it? You can start collecting.î

Ronís eyes strayed to the pile of Chocolate Frogs waiting to be unwrapped.

ìHelp yourself,î said Harry. ìBut in, you know, the Muggle world, people just stay put in photos.î

ìDo they? What, they donít move at all?î Ron sounded amazed. ìWeird!î

Harry stared as Dumbledore sidled back into the picture on his card and gave him a small smile. Ron was more interested in eating the frogs than looking at the Famous Witches and Wizards cards, but Harry couldnít keep his eyes off them. Soon he had not only Dumbledore and Morgana, but Hengist of Woodcroft, Alberic Grunnion, Circe, Paracelsus, and Merlin. He finally tore his eyes away from the druidess Cliodna, who was scratching her nose, to open a bag of Bertie Bottís Every Flavor Beans.

ìYou want to be careful with those,î Ron warned Harry. ìWhen they say every flavor, they mean every flavoróyou know, you get all the ordinary ones like chocolate and peppermint and marmalade, but then you can get spinach and liver and tripe. George reckons he had a booger-flavored one once.î

Ron picked up a green bean, looked at it carefully, and bit into a corner.

ìBleaaarghósee? Sprouts.î

They had a good time eating the Every Flavor Beans. Harry got toast, coconut, baked bean, strawberry, curry, grass, coffee, sardine, and was even brave enough to nibble the end off a funny gray one Ron wouldnít touch, which turned out to be pepper.

The countryside now flying past the window was becoming wilder. The neat fields had gone. Now there were woods, twisting rivers, and dark green hills.

There was a knock on the door of their compartment and the round faced boy Harry had passed on platform nine and threequarters came in. He looked tearful.

ìSorry,î he said, ìbut have you seen a toad at all?î

When they shook their heads, he wailed, ìIíve lost him! He keeps getting away from me!î

ìHeíll turn up,î said Harry.

ìYes,î said the boy miserably. ìWell, if you see himÖî

He left.

ìDonít know why heís so bothered,î said Ron. ìIf Iíd brought a toad Iíd lose it as quick as I could. Mind you, I brought Scabbers, so I canít talk.î

The rat was still snoozing on Ronís lap.

ìHe might have died and you wouldnít know the difference,î said Ron in disgust. ìI tried to turn him yellow yesterday to make him more interesting, but the spell didnít work. Iíll show you, lookÖî

He rummaged around in his trunk and pulled out a very battered looking wand. It was chipped in places and something white was glinting at the end.

ìUnicorn hairís nearly poking out. Anywayóî

He had just raised his wand when the compartment door slid open again. The toadless boy was back, but this time he had a girl with him. She was already wearing her new Hogwarts robes.

ìHas anyone seen a toad? Nevilleís lost one,î she said. She had a bossy sort of voice, lots of bushy brown hair, and rather large front teeth.

ìWeíve already told him we havenít seen it,î said Ron, but the girl wasnít listening, she was looking at the wand in his hand.

ìOh, are you doing magic? Letís see it, then.î

She sat down. Ron looked taken aback.

ìEróall right.î

He cleared his throat.

		ìSunshine, daisies, butter mellow,
		Turn this stupid, fat rat yellow.î

He waved his wand, but nothing happened. Scabbers stayed gray and fast asleep.

ìAre you sure thatís a real spell?î said the girl. ìWell, itís not very good, is it? Iíve tried a few simple spells just for practice and itís all worked for me. Nobody in my familyís magic at all, it was ever such a surprise when I got my letter, but I was ever so pleased, of course, I mean, itís the very best school of witchcraft there is, Iíve heardóIíve learned all our course books by heart, of course, I just hope it will be enoughóIím Hermione Granger, by the way, who are you?î

She said all this very fast.

Harry looked at Ron, and was relieved to see by his stunned face that he hadnít learned all the course books by heart either.

ìIím Ron Weasley,î Ron muttered.

ìHarry Potter,î said Harry.

ìAre you really?î said Hermione. ìI know all about you, of courseóI got a few extra books for background reading, and youíre in Modern Magical History and The Rise and Fall of the Dark Arts and Great Wizarding Events of the Twentieth Century.î

ìAm I?î said Harry, feeling dazed.

ìGoodness, didnít you know, Iíd have found out everything I could if it was me,î said Hermione. ìDo either of you know what house youíll be in? Iíve been asking around, and I hope Iím in Gryffindor, it sounds by far the best; I hear Dumbledore himself was in it, but I suppose Ravenclaw wouldnít be too badÖ Anyway, weíd better go and look for Nevilleís toad. You two had better change, you know, I expect weíll be there soon.î

And she left, taking the toadless boy with her.

ìWhatever house Iím in, I hope sheís not in it,î said Ron. He threw his wand back into his trunk. ìStupid spellóGeorge gave it to me, bet he knew it was a dud.î

ìWhat house are your brothers in?î asked Harry.

ìGryffindor,î said Ron. Gloom seemed to be settling on him again. ìMom and Dad were in it, too. I donít know what theyíll say if Iím not. I donít suppose Ravenclaw would be too bad, but imagine if they put me in Slytherin.î

ìThatís the house Voló, I mean, You-Know-Who was in?î

ìYeah,î said Ron. He flopped back into his seat, looking depressed.

ìYou know, I think the ends of Scabbersí whiskers are a bit lighter,î said Harry, trying to take Ronís mind off houses. ìSo what do your oldest brothers do now that theyíve left, anyway?î

Harry was wondering what a wizard did once heíd finished school.

ìCharlieís in Romania studying dragons, and Billís in Africa doing something for Gringotts,î said Ron. ìDid you hear about Gringotts? Itís been all over the Daily Prophet, but I donít suppose you get that with the Mugglesósomeone tried to rob a high security vault.î

Harry stared.

ìReally? What happened to them?î

ìNothing, thatís why itís such big news. They havenít been caught. My dad says it mustíve been a powerful Dark wizard to get round Gringotts, but they donít think they took anything, thatís whatís odd. íCourse, everyone gets scared when something like this happens in case You-Know-Whoís behind it.î

Harry turned this news over in his mind. He was starting to get a prickle of fear every time You-Know-Who was mentioned. He supposed this was all part of entering the magical world, but it had been a lot more comfortable saying ìVoldemortî without worrying.

ìWhatís your favorite Quidditch team?î Ron asked.

ìEróI donít know any,î Harry confessed.

ìWhat!î Ron looked dumbfounded. ìOh, you wait, itís the best game in the worldóî And he was off, explaining all about the four balls and the positions of the seven players, describing famous games heíd been to with his brothers and the broomstick heíd like to get if he had the money. He was just taking Harry through the finer points of the game when the compartment door slid open yet again, but it wasnít Neville the toadless boy, or Hermione Granger this time.

Three boys entered, and Harry recognized the middle one at once: it was the pale boy from Madam Malkinís robe shop. He was looking at Harry with a lot more interest than heíd shown back in Diagon Alley.

ìIs it true?î he said. ìTheyíre saying all down the train that Harry Potterís in this compartment. So itís you, is it?î

ìYes,î said Harry. He was looking at the other boys. Both of them were thickset and looked extremely mean. Standing on either side of the pale boy, they looked like bodyguards.

ìOh, this is Crabbe and this is Goyle,î said the pale boy carelessly, noticing where Harry was looking. ìAnd my nameís Malfoy, Draco Malfoy.î

Ron gave a slight cough, which might have been hiding a snigget. Draco Malfoy looked at him.

ìThink my nameís funny, do you? No need to ask who you are. My father told me all the Weasleys have red hair, freckles, and more children than they can afford.î

He turned back to Harry. ìYouíll soon find out some wizarding families are much better than others, Potter. You donít want to go making friends with the wrong sort. I can help you there.î

He held out his hand to shake Harryís, but Harry didnít take it.

ìI think I can tell who the wrong sort are for myself, thanks,î he said coolly.

Draco Malfoy didnít go red, but a pink tinge appeared in his pale cheeks.

ìIíd be careful if I were you, Potter,î he said slowly. ìUnless youíre a bit politer youíll go the same way as your parents. They didnít know what was good for them, either. You hang around with riffraff like the Weasleys and that Hagrid, and itíll rub off on you.î

Both Harry and Ron stood up.

ìSay that again,î Ron said, his face as red as his hair.

ìOh, youíre going to fight us, are you?î Malfoy sneered.

ìUnless you get out now,î said Harry, more bravely than he felt, because Crabbe and Goyle were a lot bigger than him or Ron.

ìBut we donít feet like leaving, do we, boys? Weíve eaten all our food and you still seem to have some.î

Goyle reached toward the Chocolate Frogs next to RonóRon leapt forward, but before heíd so much as touched Goyle, Goyle let out a horrible yell.

Scabbers the rat was hanging off his finger, sharp little teeth sunk deep into Goyleís knuckleóCrabbe and Malfoy backed away as Goyle swung Scabbers round and round, howling, and when Scabbers finally flew off and hit the window, all three of them disappeared at once. Perhaps they thought there were more rats lurking among the sweets, or perhaps theyíd heard footsteps, because a second later, Hermione Granger had come in.

ìWhat has been going on?î she said, looking at the sweets all over the floor and Ron picking up Scabbers by his tail.

ìI think heís been knocked out,î Ron said to Harry. He looked closer at Scabbers. ìNoóI donít believe itóheís gone back to sleepóî

And so he had.

ìYouíve met Malfoy before?î

Harry explained about their meeting in Diagon Alley.

ìIíve heard of his family,î said Ron darkly. ìThey were some of the first to come back to our side after You-Know-Who disappeared. Said theyíd been bewitched. My dad doesnít believe it. He says Malfoyís father didnít need an excuse to go over to the Dark Side.î He turned to Hermione. ìCan we help you with something?î

ìYouíd better hurry up and put your robes on, Iíve just been up to the front to ask the conductor, and he says weíre nearly there. You havenít been fighting, have you? Youíll be in trouble before we even get there!î

ìScabbers has been fighting, not us,î said Ron, scowling at her. ìWould you mind leaving while we change?î

ìAll rightóI only came in here because people outside are behaving very childishly, racing up and down the corridors,î said Hermione in a sniffy voice. ìAnd youíve got dirt on your nose, by the way, did you know?î

Ron glared at her as she left. Harry peered out of the window. It was getting dark. He could see mountains and forests under a deep purple sky. The train did seem to be slowing down.

He and Ron took off their jackets and pulled on their long black robes. Ronís were a bit short for him, you could see his sneakers underneath them.

A voice echoed through the train: ìWe will be reaching Hogwarts in five minutesí time. Please leave your luggage on the train, it will be taken to the school separately.î

Harryís stomach lurched with nerves and Ron, he saw, looked pale under his freckles. They crammed their pockets with the last of the sweets and joined the crowd thronging the corridor.

The train slowed right down and finally stopped. People pushed their way toward the door and out on to a tiny, dark platform. Harry shivered in the cold night air. Then a lamp came bobbing over the heads of the students, and Harry heard a familiar voice: ìFirsí years! Firsí years over here! All right there, Harry?î

Hagridís big hairy face beamed over the sea of heads.

ìCímon, follow meóany more firsí years? Mind yer step, now! Firsí years follow me!î

Slipping and stumbling, they followed Hagrid down what seemed to be a steep, narrow path. It was so dark on either side of them that Harry thought there must be thick trees there. Nobody spoke much. Neville, the boy who kept losing his toad, sniffed once or twice.

ìYeí all get yer firsí sight oí Hogwarts in a sec,î Hagrid called over his shoulder, ìjusí round this bend here.î

There was a loud ìOooooh!î

The narrow path had opened suddenly onto the edge of a great black take. Perched atop a high mountain on the other side, its windows sparkling in the starry sky, was a vast castle with many turrets and towers.

ìNo moreín four to a boat!î Hagrid called, pointing to a fleet of little boats sitting in the water by the shore. Harry and Ron were followed into their boat by Neville and Hermione.

ìEveryone in?î shouted Hagrid, who had a boat to himself. ìRight thenóFORWARD!î

And the fleet of little boats moved off all at once, gliding across the lake, which was as smooth as glass. Everyone was silent, staring up at the great castle overhead. It towered over them as they sailed nearer and nearer to the cliff on which it stood.

ìHeads down!î yelled Hagrid as the first boats reached the cliff; they all bent their heads and the little boats carried them through a curtain of ivy that hid a wide opening in the cliff face. They were carried along a dark tunnel, which seemed to be taking them right underneath the castle, until they reached a kind of underground harbor, where they clambered out onto rocks and pebbles.

ìOy, you there! Is this your toad?î said Hagrid, who was checking the boats as people climbed out of them.

ìTrevor!î cried Neville blissfully, holding out his hands. Then they clambered up a passageway in the rock after Hagridís lamp, coming out at last onto smooth, damp grass right in the shadow of the castle.

They walked up a flight of stone steps and crowded around the huge, oak front door.

ìEveryone here? You there, still got yer toad?î

Hagrid raised a gigantic fist and knocked three times on the castle door.




7. THE SORTING HAT


The door swung open at once. A tall, black haired witch in emerald green robes stood there. She had a very stern face and Harryís first thought was that this was not someone to cross.

ìThe firsí years, Professor McGonagall,î said Hagrid.

ìThank you, Hagrid. I will take them from here.î

She pulled the door wide. The entrance hall was so big you could have fit the whole of the Dursleysí house in it. The stone walls were lit with flaming torches like the ones at Gringotts, the ceiling was too high to make out, and a magnificent marble staircase facing them led to the upper floors.

They followed Professor McGonagall across the flagged stone floor.
